Refuting the Analogy Between Creator and Speaker
Maturidi refutes the analogy between khaliq and qa'il using the witnessed/unseen distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God is Creator of all but not Speaker of all speech, using the distinction between witnessed and unseen.

Core Idea
God's names are not derived from His acts in creation the same way.

Maturidi engages a Mu'tazilite argument that since God creates all actions and speech, He should be called Speaker of all speech just as He is Creator of all. This passage presents his refutation, grounded in the fundamental distinction between the witnessed world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). By the end, you will see why the name 'Qa'il' is not applied to every speech, preserving God's unique attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation to understand the overall argument.
The passage is a refutation, so expect a dialectical structure.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
Notice that Maturidi begins by referencing an opponent (likely al-Ka'bi) who draws an analogy between 'Creator' and 'Speaker'. Understand the opponent's claim: if God is called Creator because He creates all actions, then He should be called Speaker because He creates all speech.
3
Trace Maturidi's refutation
Follow how Maturidi first points out a flaw in the analogy using the shahid/gha'ib distinction. Then he states the accepted position: God is Creator of everything, but not Speaker of every speech. Then he provides a further argument based on the term 'moving'.
Notice the logical progression: flaw → position → additional proof.
4
Grasp the underlying reason
Conclude with the key point: the meaning that makes one a 'Speaker' for every speech does not exist in God. Therefore, the two names are not analogous.
This is the core theological insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلان وفلان فلئن كان ذا أصله فيجب به جواز الآخر، إذ به يلزم الآخر . ولو كان [كل] ما يجوز في الشاهد هو دليل الغائب ليجب التفريق بين الفعل والقول في الغائب كما وجب في الشاهد، وهذا يبين وهمه. ثم جائز القول بأن الله خالق كل شيء، وهو خالق وما سواه مخلوق؛ ولا يجوز أن يقال: هو قائل كل قول، ولا مخبر كل خبر، ولا هو مخبر وقائل، وما سواه خبر وقول، فدل أن أحدهما ليس بنظير للآخر. مع ما يجوز عندهم فعل كل واحد بقدرة هي فعل الله تعالى، ثم لم يجز في قول كل أحد وخبره أنه بقدرة هي قول الله تعالى وخبره. ويُقال له : إذا لم يُسم هو متحركاً بما حرّك غيره، فقل أيضاً إنه لا يُسمّى خالقا بما خلق حركة غيره؛ وإذ فصل بينهما بالعموم والخصوص أو بما شئت فافصل بينهما [به]. على أن المعنى الذي به سمى في فعل كل أحد، والمعنى الذي به سمى قائلاً لم يوجد؛ لذلك اختلفا. والله أعلم.
So-and-so and so-and-so: if the origin of this is valid, then the permissibility of the other follows, because the other is necessitated by it. And if everything that is permissible in the witnessed world is evidence for the unseen, then it would be necessary to make a distinction between action and speech in the unseen as is necessitated in the witnessed, and this shows his error. Then it is permissible to say that God is the Creator of everything, and He is the Creator and what is other than Him is created; and it is not permissible to say: He is the Speaker of every saying, nor the Informer of every report, nor is He Informer and Speaker, and what is other than Him is report and speech. So this indicates that one of them is not analogous to the other. Along with the fact that according to them the act of each one is permissible by a power which is the act of God Almighty, yet it is not permissible regarding the speech and report of everyone that it is by a power which is the speech and report of God Almighty. And it is said to him: If He is not called 'moving' by what He moved of others, then say likewise that He is not called 'Creator' by what He created of the movement of others. And since you differentiate between them by generality and specificity or by whatever you wish, differentiate between them [by it]. However, the meaning by which He is named in the act of everyone, and the meaning by which He is named 'Speaker' does not exist; therefore they differ. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an analogy made by the Mu'tazilites (specifically al-Ka'bi) between the divine names 'Creator' and 'Speaker'. Maturidi begins by pointing out the flaw in the opponent's example: if the opponent's analogy were valid, then distinctions made in the witnessed world (between action and speech) would have to apply to God, which they do not. He then states the accepted position: God is Creator of everything, but not Speaker of every speech; speech belongs to creatures. The distinction is drawn by noting that actions can be attributed to a power that is God's act, but speech cannot be similarly attributed. Further, if God is not called 'moving' by the movement He creates in others, then likewise He should not be called 'Creator' of the movement of others—indicating that the name 'Creator' is not derived from the act in the same way. The underlying reason is that the meaning by which one is named 'Creator' in the case of everyone (i.e., God is Creator of all acts) is present, but the meaning by which one is named 'Speaker' for every speech does not exist in God. Therefore, the two names are not analogous.

Key Terms
خالق
قائل
غائب
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since God creates all speech, He should be called Speaker of that speech.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the meaning of 'Speaker' (eternal attribute) is not present in created speech; God is Speaker only of His own eternal speech.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is God not called 'Qa'il of every qawl'?
A) Because He does not create all speech
B) Because the meaning that makes one a Speaker does not exist in God for every speech
C) Because speech is not created by God
D) Because the term Qa'il is only for humans

Key Takeaway
God is Creator of all, but not Speaker of all speech, because the meaning that warrants the name 'Speaker' is not present in created speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time you described God using a name or attribute in a way that might have implied a similarity to creation. What was that name, and how does this lesson challenge that understanding? Are you willing to humble your intellect before revelation and let God define Himself?
?
If God is not called 'Speaker' of every speech, what does that mean for how you approach the Quran? Do you treat it as merely a created book, or do you experience it as the very speech of God, distinct from all other speech? Let this shift how you listen to it today.
?
The passage teaches that the meaning that warrants the name 'Creator' is present in God, but the meaning for 'Speaker' is not in created speech. This means there is a part of God's essence we cannot comprehend through His creation. Does your heart rest in the mystery of God, or do you try to contain Him in human categories? Commit to one specific way you will trust His uniqueness today.

The Absurdity of Demanding 'Huwa' in Every Name
Maturidi points out the absurdity of demanding 'Huwa' in every name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why demanding the explicit pronoun 'Huwa' in universal names is logically absurd, as shown by Maturidi's analogy.

Core Idea
Requiring 'Huwa' in every name would lead to absurdities, as shown by scholars, lords, and kings.

Maturidi begins his refutation by calling the objection 'strange'. He points out that if one demanded the pronoun 'Huwa' in every absolute name, one would have to mention it when referring to scholars, doers, lords, and kings—which is clearly nonsensical. This lesson examines that initial rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic phrase where the objector claims that since 'Huwa' is not mentioned in 'Lord of everything', not everything is created by God.
2
Maturidi's first response
Notice how Maturidi calls this 'strange' and asks when 'Huwa' is ever mentioned in absolute names.
This is a rhetorical question highlighting the impracticality.
3
The absurd analogy
See how Maturidi extends the logic: if the objector were consistent, he would also require 'Huwa' when mentioning scholars, doers, lords, and kings.
This shows the objection is self-defeating.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the objection is based on a flawed expectation that does not hold in normal language or reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: «لم يدخل «هو» فيه عجيب. متى يُذكر «هو» في اسم الأشياء بالإطلاق؟ ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن يذكر في ذكر العلماء وذكر الفاعلين وذكر الوكلاء والأرباب والملوك، وذلك كلام من لا يعقل ما يقول. وبعد، فلو كان يُذكر - وإن كان ممتنعا ذلك في العقل - في الشيء لم يجز خروج غيره بخروجه لوجوه أحدها قوله: وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وهو رب كل شيء، وإله كل شيء؛ لم يجز خروج شيء من ذلك وتخصيصه في الخلق ليبطل معرفة المراد من حيث لم يدخل «هو» فيه. والثاني أنه امتداح، وفي خروجه سقوطه؛ إذ هو امتداح بما صير كل شيء تحت القدرة، وحقق في كل العبودة، وتحقيق ذلك فيه إبطال ذلك. والله الموفق. والثالث أن القول المصروف بالفعل إلى آخر والربوبية ونحو ذلك راجع إلى ذلك الآخر. وإذا كان كذلك فكأنه قال: «سواي»، ولم يكن بمثله التخصيص، فمثله الأول. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And his saying: 'He (Huwa) has not entered into it' is strange. When is 'Huwa' mentioned in the name of things absolutely? And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to mention it in mentioning the scholars, the doers, the guardians, the lords, and the kings, and that is the speech of one who does not understand what he says. Moreover, even if it were mentioned—though that is impossible in reason—in the thing, it would not permit the exclusion of other than it by its exclusion, for reasons: First, His saying: 'He is over everything a guardian, He is the Lord of everything, and the God of everything'; it is not permissible to exclude anything from that and specify it in creation so that the understanding of what is meant would be nullified because 'Huwa' has not entered into it. Second, it is praise, and in its exclusion is its fall; for it is praise by making everything under power, and actualizing in everything servitude, and actualizing that nullifies that. And Allah is the grantor of success. Third, the statement directed to another regarding the act, lordship, and the like returns to that other. And if that is so, it is as if He said: 'except me', and specification would not be like it, so the first is like it. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector (likely al-Ka'bi) claimed that the verse 'Lord of everything' does not include the pronoun 'Huwa' (He), implying that maybe not everything is created by God. Maturidi responds by calling this claim 'strange'. He asks: when is 'Huwa' used in absolute names of things? If that were allowed, it would also have to be used when mentioning scholars, doers, guardians, lords, and kings—which is senseless. Then he gives three reasons why even if 'Huwa' were mentioned (though impossible), it would not permit exclusion: First, the verse itself already says 'He is over everything a guardian, He is the Lord of everything, and the God of everything'—so nothing can be excluded even if 'Huwa' is not repeated in the first part. Second, the verse is praise (imtidaah); if anything were excluded, the praise would fall because it is praise for bringing everything under power and servitude. Third, any statement that attributes an act or lordship to another person returns in its meaning to that person; thus if God said 'except me', that would not justify specification, so the first argument stands. The conclusion is that the universality of God's creation remains intact.

Key Terms
هو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The absence of the pronoun 'Huwa' in a verse limits its meaning and allows for exceptions.
Correction: Maturidi shows that demanding 'Huwa' in every universal statement leads to absurdity and is not required by reason or language.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi call the objector's claim that 'Huwa' has not entered into it?
A) Strange
B) Reasonable
C) Praise
D) Clear

Key Takeaway
The objection that the omission of 'Huwa' allows exclusion is refuted by showing its logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a theological objection that seems small or linguistic, do you instantly reject it or examine its premises? How does Maturidi’s response teach you to handle doubts that seem trivial but could mislead your understanding of God?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Allah that you have never tested with logical consistency. Are you willing to apply the same reductio method to your own reasoning to see if it leads to absurdity? If it does, will you abandon it?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's argument as 'speech of one who does not understand' without first considering whether your own position holds up under logical extension? How can you ensure you are not the one failing to understand?

First and Second Reasons: Universal Verses and Praise
Maturidi gives the first two reasons for the non-exclusionary effect of omitted 'Huwa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first two reasons for why omitting 'Huwa' does not permit limiting God's creation: the universal verses and the praise aspect.

Core Idea
The universal verses exclude any specification, and the verse is praise that would collapse if anything were excluded.

In this lesson, we examine the first two reasons Maturidi gives for why even if 'Huwa' were mentioned (though impossible), it would not allow exclusion. The first reason appeals to other verses where 'Huwa' is explicitly mentioned universally. The second reason shows that the verse is praise, and excluding anything would negate that praise.

Guided Path
1
Read the first reason
Read where Maturidi quotes the verse: 'He is over everything a guardian, He is the Lord of everything, and the God of everything'. Notice the universality.
2
Understand the implication
Because these verses explicitly state 'He is over everything', no thing can be excluded even if 'Huwa' was omitted in the previous phrase.
3
Read the second reason
Maturidi says the verse is praise (imtidaah). If anything were excluded, the praise would be lost.
Praise requires encompassment.
4
Connect the reasons
Both reasons lead to the same conclusion: the universality of God's lordship and creation is intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: «لم يدخل «هو» فيه عجيب. متى يُذكر «هو» في اسم الأشياء بالإطلاق؟ ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن يذكر في ذكر العلماء وذكر الفاعلين وذكر الوكلاء والأرباب والملوك، وذلك كلام من لا يعقل ما يقول. وبعد، فلو كان يُذكر - وإن كان ممتنعا ذلك في العقل - في الشيء لم يجز خروج غيره بخروجه لوجوه أحدها قوله: وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وهو رب كل شيء، وإله كل شيء؛ لم يجز خروج شيء من ذلك وتخصيصه في الخلق ليبطل معرفة المراد من حيث لم يدخل «هو» فيه. والثاني أنه امتداح، وفي خروجه سقوطه؛ إذ هو امتداح بما صير كل شيء تحت القدرة، وحقق في كل العبودة، وتحقيق ذلك فيه إبطال ذلك. والله الموفق. والثالث أن القول المصروف بالفعل إلى آخر والربوبية ونحو ذلك راجع إلى ذلك الآخر. وإذا كان كذلك فكأنه قال: «سواي»، ولم يكن بمثله التخصيص، فمثله الأول. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And his saying: 'He (Huwa) has not entered into it' is strange. When is 'Huwa' mentioned in the name of things absolutely? And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to mention it in mentioning the scholars, the doers, the guardians, the lords, and the kings, and that is the speech of one who does not understand what he says. And if that is so, it is as if He said: 'except me', and specification would not be like it, so the first is like it. And there is no power except with Allah.

What is the second reason given by Maturidi for why omitting 'Huwa' does not allow exclusion?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that any exclusion of something from God's power nullifies the praise due to Him. Is there an area of your life—your career, relationships, or inner struggles—where you act as if it is outside God's control? How will you consciously affirm God's lordship over that area today?
?
The universal verses declare God as 'Lord of everything' and 'God of everything.' Do you truly believe that God is the Lord of every detail in your life, including your failures and disappointments? If not, what would change if you started seeing them as under His authority?
?
Maturidi argues that even hypothetically adding 'Huwa' does not allow exceptions. This means no part of creation escapes God's dominion. When you find yourself making exceptions—thinking 'this is too small for God to care' or 'God wouldn't involve Himself here'—what does that reveal about your heart's true view of God's sovereignty? Commit to one specific area where you will stop making exceptions this week.

Third Reason: The Implication of 'Except Me'
Maturidi gives the third reason and concludes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third reason: that any statement attributing an act or lordship to another implicitly returns to that other, so when God says 'except me', it does not justify specification.

Core Idea
If God said 'except me', it would not create a specification, so the first argument stands.

Maturidi's third reason is more subtle. He argues that when a statement directs an act or lordship to another, the meaning ultimately returns to that other. Thus, if God were to say 'except me', it would not create a valid specification, and the universal lordship remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Read the third reason
Read Maturidi's statement: 'The statement directed to another regarding the act, lordship, and the like returns to that other.'
2
Understand the logic
If someone says 'except me' in a context of universal lordship, it would be a self-reference that does not create a true exception.
3
Final conclusion
Maturidi concludes that specification is not possible, so the first argument (the universal verses) stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: «لم يدخل «هو» فيه عجيب. متى يُذكر «هو» في اسم الأشياء بالإطلاق؟ ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن يذكر في ذكر العلماء وذكر الفاعلين وذكر الوكلاء والأرباب والملوك، وذلك كلام من لا يعقل ما يقول. وبعد، فلو كان يُذكر - وإن كان ممتنعا ذلك في العقل - في الشيء لم يجز خروج غيره بخروجه لوجوه أحدها قوله: وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وهو رب كل شيء، وإله كل شيء؛ لم يجز خروج شيء من ذلك وتخصيصه في الخلق ليبطل معرفة المراد من حيث لم يدخل «هو» فيه. والثاني أنه امتداح، وفي خروجه سقوطه؛ إذ هو امتداح بما صير كل شيء تحت القدرة، وحقق في كل العبودة، وتحقيق ذلك فيه إبطال ذلك. والله الموفق. والثالث أن القول المصروف بالفعل إلى آخر والربوبية ونحو ذلك راجع إلى ذلك الآخر. وإذا كان كذلك فكأنه قال: «سواي»، ولم يكن بمثله التخصيص، فمثله الأول. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The Impossibility of Anything Outside God's Creation
Maturidi establishes the principle that any exception to God's creation would destroy divine praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that if anything were outside God's creation, divine praise would be invalidated.

Core Idea
Every exception to God's creation makes Him a partner in His own praise.

Maturidi begins by warning against any thought that something could exist independently of God's creative act. He shows that such an exception would unravel all divine titles of praise, because every title would then be shared.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the Arabic passage from the start to 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'. Notice the logical chain: if one exception is allowed, then the same exception applies to all names of praise.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Connect to the concept of praise
Maturidi says that if God had partners in the meaning of His attributes, He would have no exclusive praise. Think about why divine praise requires that God alone be the true possessor of those attributes.
This principle is foundational for tawhid.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Consider the vast implication: the very possibility of something being outside God's creation would erode all divine praise and majesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز خروج شيء من أن يكون هو له خالقا لجاز مثله عن الملك والربوبية ونحو ذلك من أسماء الامتداح، فيبطل أن يكون له مدح بشيء، لما في كل شيء له شركاء في حقيقة معناه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقوله: «لم يدخل «هو» فيه عجيب. متى يُذكر «هو» في اسم الأشياء بالإطلاق؟ ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن يذكر في ذكر العلماء وذكر الفاعلين وذكر الوكلاء والأرباب والملوك، وذلك كلام من لا يعقل ما يقول. وبعد، فلو كان يُذكر - وإن كان ممتنعا ذلك في العقل - في الشيء لم يجز خروج غيره بخروجه لوجوه أحدها قوله: وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وهو رب كل شيء، وإله كل شيء؛ لم يجز خروج شيء من ذلك وتخصيصه في الخلق ليبطل معرفة المراد من حيث لم يدخل «هو» فيه. والثاني أنه امتداح، وفي خروجه سقوطه؛ إذ هو امتداح بما صير كل شيء تحت القدرة، وحقق في كل العبودة، وتحقيق ذلك فيه إبطال ذلك. والله الموفق. والثالث أن القول المصروف بالفعل إلى آخر والربوبية ونحو ذلك راجع إلى ذلك الآخر. وإذا كان كذلك فكأنه قال: «سواي»، ولم يكن بمثله التخصيص، فمثله الأول.
And if it were permissible for anything to come out from being such that He is its Creator, then the like would be permissible concerning [the names] King, Lord, and similar names of praise, and thus it would invalidate that He has praise through anything, because in everything He has partners in the reality of its meaning. And there is no power except with God. And his saying: 'He did not enter 'Huwa' ('He') into it' is amazing. When is 'Huwa' mentioned in the name of things absolutely? And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to mention it in mentioning the scholars, the agents, the deputies, the lords, and the kings, and that is the speech of one who does not understand what he says. Moreover, if it were mentioned – even if that is impossible in reason – in the thing, it would not be permissible for others to come out by its coming out, for several reasons: first, His saying: 'And He is over all things a Guardian, and He is the Lord of everything, and the God of everything'; it is not permissible for anything to come out from that and be specified in creation, so as to invalidate the knowledge of the intended because 'Huwa' did not enter into it. Second, that it is praise, and in its coming out is its downfall; for it is praise for having placed everything under power and realized servitude in everything, and realizing that entails nullifying that. And God is the Granter of success. Third, that the saying directed by the act to another, and lordship and the like, refers back to that other. And if that is so, it is as if He said: 'other than Me', and specification is not like that, so it is like the first.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument against an opponent who claims that some things (like acts of disobedience) are outside God's creation because the word 'Huwa' (He) is not explicitly mentioned in descriptions of those things. Maturidi first states a general principle: if it were possible for anything to be outside God's creative power, then the same exception could be made for God's names like King and Lord, which would destroy all divine praise because everything would have partners in the meaning of those names. He then turns to the opponent's specific claim about 'Huwa', calling it absurd. He asks when 'Huwa' is ever used in naming things absolutely; if it were, it would also apply to human scholars, agents, lords, and kings—which is clearly nonsensical. Then he gives three reasons why even if 'Huwa' were mentioned in a thing, that would not allow excluding other things from God's power. First, the Quran explicitly says God is Guardian, Lord, and God of everything, so nothing can be specified out. Second, these names are praise, and excluding anything from them would negate that praise because they imply universal power and servitude. Third, if you attribute lordship and actions to something else, that attribution refers to that other entity, so if you say 'other than Me', specification fails. Thus the opponent's use of 'Huwa' does not help his case.

Key Terms
خالق (Creator)
ملك (King)
رب (Lord)
امتداح (praise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if something were outside God's creation, what would be the consequence for God's names of praise?
A) They would still be valid because God is still the Creator of everything else.
B) They would be invalidated because God would have partners in the meaning of those names.
C) They would apply only to the things within creation.
D) They would become metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Allowing any exception to God's creation would destroy the exclusive praise due to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any area of your life where you secretly believe God is not the ultimate Creator? How would that make your praise of Him incomplete? Will you repent and acknowledge His exclusive creation over all things, including your own actions?
?
If you attribute any success or goodness to your own effort or to nature apart from God, are you not making Him a partner in His own praise? Examine your heart: do you truly see God as the sole source of every blessing? What will you change today to correct this?
?
When you say 'Alhamdulillah', do you mean it for every single thing, or are there exceptions you reserve for your own doing? Let this argument expose the inconsistency in your gratitude, and commit to a praise that leaves no room for partners.

The Invalidity of the Opponent's Use of 'Huwa'
Maturidi refutes the opponent's use of 'Huwa' with a reductio and three logical points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three-fold refutation of the opponent's argument that the word 'Huwa' ('He') can be used to exclude things from God's creation.

Core Idea
The opponent's appeal to 'Huwa' is absurd and self-defeating.

Having established the general principle, Maturidi now addresses the specific argument of his opponent, who claims that because the word 'Huwa' is not explicitly mentioned in descriptions of certain things (like acts of disobedience), those things fall outside God's creation. Maturidi demolishes this claim by pointing out its absurd implications and then providing three logical reasons why even if 'Huwa' were used, it would not help.

Guided Path
1
Read the absurdity of 'Huwa'
Read the part from 'وقوله: لم يدخل هو فيه عجيب' to 'كلام من لا يعقل ما يقول'. Notice how Maturidi shows that using 'Huwa' absolutely would apply to humans as well, which is nonsensical.
This is a reductio ad absurdum on the opponent's own method.
2
Study the three reasons
Read the three reasons given after 'وبعد، فلو كان يُذكر'. Each reason shows why even if 'Huwa' were used in a thing, other things cannot be excluded.
The reasons are based on Quranic texts and logical consistency.
3
Summarise the argument
Maturidi concludes that the opponent's use of 'Huwa' fails to achieve his goal. Understand how each reason builds on the previous.
The third reason ties back to the first.

Refuting the Claim that God's Creating Insults Shames Him
Maturidi refutes the charge that if God creates an insult, He would be insulted; he distinguishes between creating an act and being characterized by it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the objection that God's creation of insulting acts would make Him insulted or blameworthy, by distinguishing between the ontological nature of a created act and its moral attribution.

Core Idea
Creating an act does not make the Creator share its moral quality.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common theological objection: if God creates an act of insult, does that not make God insulted? In this passage, he clarifies that the act of insult, considered as a created entity—an accident or movement—is not inherently insulting. The moral quality belongs to the agent who commits the act, not to the Creator. This distinction is crucial for preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: Maturidi first quotes the objection, then refutes it, then provides an analogy.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that if God creates an insult, He would be insulting Himself and thus blameworthy. Locate where Maturidi dismisses this as false.
Look for the phrase 'وما ذكر من أنه شتم نفسه'.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi argues that the act of insult, when created by God, is ontologically neutral—it is an accident or movement. Its moral quality (insult) applies only to the disbeliever who performs it. Trace his analogy about knowing and reporting.
The key is distinguishing the act as a created thing from its moral description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من أنه شتم نفسه و كفر به ونحو هذا، فهو الذي لم يزل يعود نفسه من الكذب على خصومه. وليس أحد منهم يقول ذلك. بل لو خلق شتم نفسه يكون مشتوما في الحقيقة مذموما. بل خلق فعل الشتم من الكافر كذبًا وجورًا وسفها، وفي ذلك دفع كونه مشتوماً مذموما في الحقيقة. ألا ترى أن من عرف فعل الشتم كذلك يكون عالما حكيما، ومن أخبره عنه كذلك يكون صادقاً؛ ومن عرفه على ما عليه عند الكافر كان جاهلاً سفيها، وبالخبر به كذلك يكون كاذبا، فمثله الذي ذكر. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وجملته أن فعله من حيث كان عرضاً أو شيئاً أو دليلاً على سفهه أو حركة ونحو ذلك لا يوصف بشتم ولا قبح، فمثله من وجه خلقه إياه.
And what he mentioned about [God] insulting Himself and disbelieving in Him and the like, this is he who has not ceased to accuse himself of lying against his opponents. And not one of them says that. Rather, if [God] created the insulting of Himself, He would actually be insulted and blamed. Rather, creating the act of insult from the disbeliever [is] lying, injustice, and foolishness, and in that there is a repulsion of His being insulted and blamed in reality. Do you not see that whoever knows the act of insult as such is knowing and wise, and whoever reports about it as such is truthful; and whoever knows it as it is according to the disbeliever is ignorant and foolish, and by reporting it as such he is a liar. So the like of what he mentioned [is false]. And there is no power except with God. And the summary: that His act, inasmuch as it is an accident or a thing or evidence of foolishness or a movement and the like, is not described as insult or ugliness, so the like of it, from the perspective of His creating it, [is the same].
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's claim that if God created the act of insult (i.e., disbelief or blasphemy), then God would be insulting Himself and thus would be blameworthy. Maturidi begins by accusing the opponent of lying, noting that no one actually makes that claim. He then argues that if God created the insult against Himself, He would indeed be insulted—but that is not the case. Rather, God creates the act of insult from the disbeliever as an act of lying, injustice, and foolishness (on the part of the disbeliever), and this very fact repels the idea that God is actually insulted. Maturidi draws an analogy: a person who understands the act of insult in its true nature (as an evil act) is wise and truthful; but one who sees it only from the perspective of the disbeliever (who thinks it is justified) is ignorant and foolish. Therefore, the act of insult, when considered as an accident or movement created by God, is not inherently insulting or ugly—it is just a created entity. Thus, the creation of such an act does not make the Creator insulted. The key point is the distinction between the ontological reality of the act (as a created thing) and its moral quality relative to the agent.

Key Terms
شتم (insult)
خلق (creation)
كافر (disbeliever)
كذب (lie)
جاهل (ignorant)
حكيم (wise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates an evil act, He becomes evil or blameworthy.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the act as a created entity—an accident or movement—is ontologically neutral; its moral quality depends on the agent who performs it, not the Creator. Therefore, God's creation of an act does not make Him share in its moral judgment.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the claim that God would be insulting Himself by creating insult?
A) By saying that God never creates such acts
B) By distinguishing the created act from the creator's character, and noting that the act as created is neutral
C) By claiming that the disbeliever's act is not really an insult
D) By denying that God creates human actions

Key Takeaway
God's creation of an act does not make Him partake in its moral quality; the act considered ontologically is neutral.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin or face a trial, do you ever internally blame God for creating the situation? How does this lesson call you to instead take full responsibility for your own choices and reactions?
?
The passage teaches that God's creation of an act does not make Him share its moral quality. Is there a sin in your life that you have been excusing by saying 'God made me this way' or 'it's my destiny'? What would it mean for you to truly own that sin before God today?
?
If you truly believed that God's actions are always wise and just, even when He creates events that seem evil to you, how would your trust in Him change in the next difficult situation you face?

The Reality of Creation in Human Acts
Maturidi introduces a rational refutation of the Mu'tazila and asserts that creation is real in human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the reality of creation (ḥaqīqa) actually lies in human acts, not directly in God, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
The reality of creation is in human acts, not in divine direct action.

Maturidi opens this passage with a sharp critique of the Mu'tazila, who claim it is impossible to attribute the reality of creation to God. He then argues that the true locus of creation is human acts. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for the debate about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the first few sentences of the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how Maturidi challenges the Mu'tazila by pointing out their inconsistency.
The Mu'tazila deny that the reality of creation can be attributed to God, yet they use transmitted proof against rational deniers.
2
Understand the logical flaw
Maturidi questions how the Mu'tazila can argue against someone who rejects their claim on rational grounds, since their own proof relies on scripture (sam'), not reason.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the reality of creation must be in the acts of human beings (al-khalq). This becomes the foundation for divine praise.
Remember, this is not about denying God's ultimate power, but about the direct attribution of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتزلة من ذلك الوجه الذي لا يوجد في أصل دينهم جواز إضافة حقيقة ذلك إلى الله. فكيف يحتج على منكر مثله ممن يزعم أن ذلك في العقل مدفوع وطريقه السمع، ومحال الاحتجاج بالسمع على إمكانه في العقل؟ ثبت أن حقيقة ذلك في أفعال الخلق، وبه يكون امتداح في الحقيقة من وجوه أحدها في جعل كل شيء بحيث القدرة تحت قدرة الله، ليظهر حاجة الخلق جملة إلى الله تعالى في كون كل شيء لهم به والثاني أن الوصف بالقدرة على ما لا فعل فيه لغيره ليس بعجيب، بل يستحقه كل ضعيف مهان، ثبت أن الامتداح يكون هذا الوجه والثالث فيه بيان سفه من يفهم أن خلق كل شيء على ما عليه وصف الرب به أو تحقيق الفعل من الوجه الذي يكون من العباد منه. والرابع ليعلم أن الله أن يلحقه ذم في فعل أو مدح، من حيث ذلك المفعول، بحال. وذلك ينقض من الاعتزال، إذ جعلوا له هذا الامتداح بخلقه. وما يكون كذلك فهو على شرف الزوال وخوف الانقطاع. جل ربنا عن ذلك.
The Mu'tazila from that aspect that there is no permission in the origin of their religion to attribute the reality of that to God. So how can one argue against a denier of the like of it who claims that this is repelled by the intellect and its path is transmission (sam'), and it is impossible to argue by transmission for its possibility in the intellect? It is established that the reality of that is in the acts of creation, and by it there is praise in reality from aspects: one of them is in making everything such that power is under the power of God, to show the need of all creation for God exalted in that everything is for them. The second is that describing with power over what no other has action in it is not wonderful, but every weak contemptible one deserves it. It is established that praise is this aspect. The third is in it an exposition of the foolishness of one who understands that the creation of everything as it is requires describing the Lord with it or the actualization of the act from the aspect from which it comes from servants. The fourth is that it may be known that God is free from blame or praise from the perspective of that effect, in any case. And that invalidates the Mu'tazila, for they made this praise [derive] from His creation. And what is like that is on the verge of passing and fear of cessation. Exalted is our Lord above that.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing that the reality of creation (i.e., true attribution of creative acts) lies in human acts, and that the Mu'tazila's position undermines divine praise. Maturidi begins by pointing out that the Mu'tazila have no basis in their religion to attribute the reality of creation to God. Then he questions how they can argue against someone who denies such attribution on rational grounds. He asserts that the reality of creation is in human acts, and for that, God deserves praise. He then lists four aspects of this praise: (1) It shows that all power is under God's power, (2) True power is over what others cannot do, (3) It exposes the folly of those who think God's description requires creation in the same manner as humans, (4) It clarifies that God is beyond blame or praise based on the effect. He concludes that the Mu'tazila's view invalidates true praise.

Key Terms
الاعتزال (al-i'tizal)
إضافة (idafa)
حقيقة (haqiqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think it is impossible to attribute creation to God, but they still claim that God creates everything. Maturidi shows they are inconsistent.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if you deny that the reality of creation can be attributed to God, then you must place it in human acts, otherwise you have no basis for divine praise.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why can't the Mu'tazila argue against a rational denier using transmitted proof?
A) Because the denier accepts reason over transmission.
B) Because the Mu'tazila have no scripture.
C) Because transmission and reason conflict.
D) Because the Mu'tazila are irrational.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that the reality (ḥaqīqa) of creation belongs to human acts, not directly to God, thereby resolving the Mu'tazila dilemma.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you truly feel the weight of creating that act—its reality—or do you attribute it to God as a mere vessel? How would acknowledging your creative responsibility change your sense of accountability?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot be the creator of evil acts. But Maturidi insists that the reality of creation is in your acts. If the creation of your actions is truly yours, how do you face that judgment? Are you prepared to own every act you create?
?
You have just read that the Mu'tazila rely on scripture to deny the reality of creation in human acts, yet they cannot argue against a rational denier with scripture alone. In your own life, do you ever rely on religious arguments without rational grounding? Are you willing to be refuted by reason?

Four Aspects of Divine Praise from Human Acts
Maturidi lists four aspects of divine praise derived from creation in human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four aspects (wujūh) of divine praise that arise from the reality of creation being in human acts, and how these refute the Mu'tazila view.

Core Idea
God's true praise comes from His power over all things, not from direct creation.

Having established that the reality of creation lies in human acts, Maturidi now enumerates four ways in which this very fact gives rise to genuine praise of God. These aspects highlight God's absolute power, expose the folly of those who equate divine and human action, and ultimately refute the Mu'tazila notion that God's praise depends on His being the direct creator of all acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the four aspects carefully
Read the Arabic enumeration of the four aspects (wujūh) one by one. Notice how each builds on the previous one.
2
Understand each aspect individually
For each aspect, identify the key point: first is about power, second about true power, third about folly, fourth about God's transcendence above praise/blame from effects.
The third aspect uses the term 'safah' (foolishness) to criticize those who equate divine and human action.
3
See the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The fourth aspect and the concluding sentence directly invalidate the Mu'tazila claim that God's praise derives from His being the direct creator of acts.
Maturidi says 'that invalidates the Mu'tazila, for they made this praise [derive] from His creation.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتزلة من ذلك الوجه الذي لا يوجد في أصل دينهم جواز إضافة حقيقة ذلك إلى الله. فكيف يحتج على منكر مثله ممن يزعم أن ذلك في العقل مدفوع وطريقه السمع، ومحال الاحتجاج بالسمع على إمكانه في العقل؟ ثبت أن حقيقة ذلك في أفعال الخلق، وبه يكون امتداح في الحقيقة من وجوه أحدها في جعل كل شيء بحيث القدرة تحت قدرة الله، ليظهر حاجة الخلق جملة إلى الله تعالى في كون كل شيء لهم به والثاني أن الوصف بالقدرة على ما لا فعل فيه لغيره ليس بعجيب، بل يستحقه كل ضعيف مهان، ثبت أن الامتداح يكون هذا الوجه والثالث فيه بيان سفه من يفهم أن خلق كل شيء على ما عليه وصف الرب به أو تحقيق الفعل من الوجه الذي يكون من العباد منه. والرابع ليعلم أن الله أن يلحقه ذم في فعل أو مدح، من حيث ذلك المفعول، بحال. وذلك ينقض من الاعتزال، إذ جعلوا له هذا الامتداح بخلقه. وما يكون كذلك فهو على شرف الزوال وخوف الانقطاع. جل ربنا عن ذلك.
The Mu'tazila from that aspect that there is no permission in the origin of their religion to attribute the reality of that to God. So how can one argue against a denier of the like of it who claims that this is repelled by the intellect and its path is transmission (sam'), and it is impossible to argue by transmission for its possibility in the intellect? It is established that the reality of that is in the acts of creation, and by it there is praise in reality from aspects: one of them is in making everything such that power is under the power of God, to show the need of all creation for God exalted in that everything is for them. The second is that describing with power over what no other has action in it is not wonderful, but every weak contemptible one deserves it. It is established that praise is this aspect. The third is in it an exposition of the foolishness of one who understands that the creation of everything as it is requires describing the Lord with it or the actualization of the act from the aspect from which it comes from servants. The fourth is that it may be known that God is free from blame or praise from the perspective of that effect, in any case. And that invalidates the Mu'tazila, for they made this praise [derive] from His creation. And what is like that is on the verge of passing and fear of cessation. Exalted is our Lord above that.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing that the reality of creation (i.e., true attribution of creative acts) lies in human acts, and that the Mu'tazila's position undermines divine praise. Maturidi begins by pointing out that the Mu'tazila have no basis in their religion to attribute the reality of creation to God. Then he questions how they can argue against someone who denies such attribution on rational grounds. He asserts that the reality of creation is in human acts, and for that, God deserves praise. He then lists four aspects of this praise: (1) It shows that all power is under God's power, (2) True power is over what others cannot do, (3) It exposes the folly of those who think God's description requires creation in the same manner as humans, (4) It clarifies that God is beyond blame or praise based on the effect. He concludes that the Mu'tazila's view invalidates true praise.

سفه (safah)

Correction: Maturidi shows that true praise comes from His absolute power over all things, not from direct creation of each act.

The four aspects demonstrate that God's true praise lies in His absolute sovereignty, not in being the direct creator of every act, thus refuting the Mu'tazila.

?
The Mu'tazila wanted God's praise to be grounded in visible outcomes—direct creation. Do you ever measure your spiritual worth by tangible results? Surrender that need for visible validation and trust that God's true praise stands on His absolute sovereignty alone.

Consensus on Negating Tashbih Through Temporality
Maturidi argues from Muslim consensus that tashbih is negated because it would entail temporality in Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Muslim consensus negates any similarity between Allah and creation based on the principle that similarity would entail temporality, which is impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Temporality (ḥadath) is the key that shows why tashbih is impossible.

Imam Maturidi presents a foundational argument from Muslim consensus: if Allah were similar to creation in any respect, that respect would carry temporality. Since temporality is a mark of contingency, negating it from Allah is essential. The passage shows the logical chain connecting temporal events (ḥawādith) in bodies to the need for a Creator, and why denying this similarity is meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the consensus
Start by noting that Maturidi invokes the consensus of Muslims to establish the negated of tashbih. This is the first premise.
Consensus (ijma) is a strong source of proof in Islamic theology.
2
Analyze the conditional logic
Notice the 'if...then' structure: if similarity occurred in terms of temporality, it would necessitate Allah's temporality. Understand that this is why similarity is negated.
The logical structure is: if X, then Y; but Y is impossible, so X is impossible.
3
Trace the proof chain
Follow the argument from temporal events in bodies → temporality of bodies → need for a non-temporal Maker → this whole chain is a sign of similarity among creatures. Each step builds on the previous.
The chain establishes that all bodies share temporality, which is the aspect of similarity.
4
Evaluate the refutation
Maturidi rejects the opposing view by calling it meaningless. Understand that denying similarity in this context is self-defeating because the entire proof rests on it.
The refutation targets those who claim no similarity arises from temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من قول المسلمين نفي تشبيه الخلق عن الله لأنه من الوجه الذي يقع فيه تشابه يوجب حدثه بحدث الشيء. فلو لم يقع من حيث الحدث تشابه لم يكن يُنفى من حيث لزوم الحدث مع ما كانت الحوادث في الأجسام هي أدلة حدثها، وحدث الأجسام هو دلالة المحدث الصانع، وذلك كله آية التشابه. فقوله : لا يقع بذا تشابه، لا معنى له.
Then from the statement of the Muslims is the negation of the similarity of creation to Allah, because it is from the aspect in which similarity occurs that necessitates His temporality by the temporality of the thing. For if similarity did not occur in terms of temporality, it would not be negated in terms of the necessity of temporality, given that the temporal events in bodies are proofs of their temporality, and the temporality of bodies is the indication of the Eternal Maker (al-muḥdith al-ṣāniʿ), and all of that is a sign of similarity. So his statement: 'No similarity occurs in this way' has no meaning.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that similarity (tashābuh) is not relevant to temporality (ḥadath). Maturidi begins by invoking the consensus of Muslims that creation cannot be compared to Allah because the aspect in which similarity occurs would necessitate that Allah is temporal (ḥādith) like the creature, which is impossible. He argues that if similarity did not occur in terms of temporality, then there would be no need to negate it. But since all bodies exhibit temporal events (ḥawādith), and these events are evidence of the bodies' temporality, and the temporality of bodies points to a Maker who is not temporal, the entire chain is a sign of similarity (i.e., the creatures share temporality). Therefore, the claim that no similarity arises from this perspective is meaningless. The target here is likely al-Kaʿbī or other Muʿtazilites who denied that similarity occurs through temporality.

Key Terms
تشبيه
حدث
حوادث
أجسام
محدث صانع
تشابه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the passage implies that Allah is temporal because similarity is negated based on temporality.
Correction: The passage actually argues the opposite: similarity would force Allah to be temporal, so Allah cannot be similar to creation. It is creatures who are temporal, not Allah.

Checkpoint

A) Their existence is self-evident.
B) They contain temporary events (ḥawādith).
C) The Qur'an says so.
D) The intellect alone cannot determine.

Key Takeaway
Muslim consensus negates all similarity between Allah and creation because similarity would imply temporality, which is impossible for the Eternal Maker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you catch yourself picturing Him with qualities you experience in creation? How does this lesson's argument that similarity would force temporality challenge those mental images? Will you now consciously replace such images with pure transcendence?

This lesson shows that Muslim consensus negates tashbih. Is there any area of your belief where you unknowingly hold a similarity between Allah and creation, even in how you speak about His attributes? What specific words will you change today?
?
The proof from temporality shows that even the most subtle similarity would imply an impossible createdness. Have you ever defended an anthropomorphic idea because it felt comforting? What would it cost you to let go of that comfort for the sake of tanzih?

Refuting al-Kaʿbī: Creation Is Not the Created
Maturidi refutes al-Kaʿbī's doctrine of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the Muʿtazilite identification of creation with the created thing, and how it preserves Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
If creation equals the created, then Allah would own evil and defect.

Maturidi engages in a polemical refutation of al-Kaʿbī's claim that 'creation is the created thing.' He reveals the absurd consequences: if creation and the created are identical, then all the imperfections found in creation—devil, evil, filth—would be directly attributed to Allah. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the strong language Maturidi uses to condemn al-Kaʿbī's view.
The passage is polemical and uses rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the absurd consequences
List the negative qualities Maturidi mentions (lowness, need, devil, etc.) and see how they would apply to Allah if creation = created.
This is the core reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the point about Iblis
Maturidi points out that Muʿtazilites would have to call Satan's creation a 'favor' and 'wisdom'—a clear contradiction. This shows their position is unreasonable.
4
Grasp the final theological principle
The passage concludes that Allah is One, and similarity/difference only occur among distinct entities (aghyār). Thus, no similarity can apply to Allah.
This reinforces divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ذا ذا ، فإن خلق الشيء عندهم هو الخلق. ولا شك أنه في الخلق ذلة وخضوع وحاجة وعيوب وشيطان وشر وفتنة وبلاء وفساد ونتن وخبث وقذر، كل هذه أوصاف فعل الله تعالى عند المعتزلة بقوله : خلق الشيء هو ذلك الشيء، فكيف أنكر هذه الأحوال خلقه ؟ وقوله : هو تطوّل وتفضل؛ فإذا إبليس هو خلقه عندهم، وفعله في الحقيقة تطوّل وتفضّل، وهو خير وحسن، وهو حكمة وصواب. وهذا كله قول وحش، لم يجز إطلاق ذلك إلا بصلات توضح المراد، فمثله الذي ذكر، وليس من هذا الوجه دفع التشابه ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم العجب ممن يعجب منه ، وفي ذلك أنه واحد، والتشابه والاختلاف أبدا يقع في الأغيار. وجملة ذلك أنا نجد فعل العبد من الوجه الذي عليه أمر العالم الله، فثبت أن
And his statement: 'This is this,' for the creation of a thing according to them is the creation. There is no doubt that in creation there is lowness, submission, need, defects, devil, evil, trial, calamity, corruption, stench, wickedness, and filth. All these are descriptions of the act of Allah the Exalted according to the Muʿtazilites by his statement: 'The creation of a thing is that same thing.' So how could he deny these states from His creation? And his statement: 'It is graciousness and favor'; then Iblis would be His creation according to them, and his act in reality would be graciousness and favor, and it is goodness and beauty, and it is wisdom and correctness. All this is a monstrous saying, not allowed to be uttered except with qualifications that clarify the intended meaning. So such is what he mentioned, and from this aspect there is no refutation of similarity, and there is no power except with Allah. Then wonder at the one who wonders, and in that He is one, and similarity and difference always occur among distinct entities. The summary is that we find the act of the servant from the aspect upon which the command of the world is from Allah, so it is established that...
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a polemical refutation of al-Kaʿbī's equation of creation with the created thing. Maturidi points out that if creation is identical to the created thing, then all the negative qualities found in creation (lowness, evil, etc.) would be attributed to Allah. The Muʿtazilites, however, claim that creation is a favor and grace, but this leads to absurd consequences: Iblis (Satan) would be a creation of Allah, and his evil actions would be described as good and wise, which is irrational. Maturidi calls this a 'monstrous saying' and argues that such statements are only permissible with explanatory qualifications (ṣilāt). He then moves to a final point: wonder is expressed at the one who finds similarity, but similarity and difference only occur among distinct entities (aghyār), meaning that since Allah is One and unique, no similarity can apply. The entire discussion concludes that the act of the servant is from the aspect that the world's command is from Allah, thus establishing divine transcendence. The incomplete final sentence likely affirms that similarity is impossible.

مخلوق
تطوّل
تفضّل
إبليس
أغيار
تشابه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah creates evil, then evil must be attributed to His essence.
Correction: Maturidi refutes that creation and the created are identical; thus, the defects in the created do not apply to the Creator's essence.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Maturidi draw from the Muʿtazilite equation of creation with the created thing?
A) That evil in creation proves Allah's mercy.
B) That negative qualities like evil and filth would be directly attributed to Allah.
C) That creation is purely good.
D) That similarity is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Identifying creation with the created thing leads to attributing imperfections to Allah, which is impossible; instead, creation is an act distinct from its product, preserving divine transcendence.

The Objection on Creating Bodies: Maturidi's Response
Maturidi redefines body creation and counters Mu'tazilite objection with Zayd argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's response to the Mu'tazilite objection that if humans create accidents, they can create bodies, and how he redefines creation and uses the Zayd argument.

Core Idea
Creating a body is the same as creating an accident: bringing from non-existence.

In this passage, Maturidi engages in a dialectical exchange with the Mu'tazila, who argue that if humans have the power to create accidents, they should also be able to create bodies. Maturidi responds by redefining the meaning of creating a body as simply bringing it from non-existence, just as with accidents. The debate unfolds with further objections and responses, culminating in the Zayd argument that highlights a crucial asymmetry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the debate. Note the different parts: initial statement, objection, redefinition, counter, and the Zayd argument.
Pay attention to the phrases 'ليس معنى خلق الجسم إلا خروجه من العدم' and 'فعل زید سوی فعل بقدرة'.
2
Identify the objection
The Mu'tazila object: if you can bring accidents from non-existence, why can't you do the same for bodies? This is the key challenge Maturidi addresses.
3
Understand Maturidi's redefinition
Maturidi states that the very meaning of creating a body is its emergence from non-existence. Therefore, the same process applies to bodies as to accidents. This reframes the debate.
He says 'ليس معنى خلق الجسم إلا خروجه من العدم ووجوده بعد أن لم يكن'.
4
Follow the counter and critique
The opponent answers with 'the action' (al-fi‘l). Maturidi critiques this by noting that we do not possess the specific aspect of a body's existence in our actions, whereas they (the Mu'tazila) do claim that for accidents.
5
Examine the Zayd argument
The Zayd argument: Zayd's action is by power, and you act by the same power. Why can't you do Zayd's action? The reply: Zayd does not give us power over his specific action, but God gave the Mu'tazila power over the meaning by which a body exists, so the objection stands against them.
This illustrates the asymmetry between human and divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأسقام لا يجوز أن يخلقها لا في أحد ولا لأحد، ثم لم يمنع تحقق الحكمة لها، فمثله الذي ذكر. والله الموفق.
ثم عارض نفسه بأنه إذا قدرتم على إخراج الأعراض من العدم إلى الوجود لم لا جاز أن تقدروا على ذلك في الجسم؟ {قال أبو منصور رحمه الله:} وليس هذا تقدير السؤال، ولكن بما ليس معنى خلق الجسم إلا خروجه من العدم ووجوده بعد أن لم يكن، وبه وصفتم أنفسكم في فعل الأعراض، كيف لا جاز الوصف بخلق الجسم وليس ثمة غير؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله.
فأجاب بالفعل. وذلك فاسد، لأنّنا لم نحقق لنا في فعلنا الوجه الذي هو وجه وجود الجسم وكونه، ليلزمنا ذلك، وهم قد حققوا فيلزمهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
ثم قال: إذ ليس فعل زید سوی فعل بقدرة وانتم تفعلون بها كيف فعلتم فعل زيد؟ قيل: لأن زيدا لا يقدرنا على فعله، فلم نفعل، والله قد أقدركم على المعنى الذي به كان الجسم، فلزمكم ما قابلناكم به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then illnesses cannot be created neither in anyone nor for anyone, yet He does not prevent the realization of wisdom for it, so it is like what was mentioned. And God is the one who grants success. Then he objected against himself: if you have power to bring accidents from non-existence into existence, why is it not permissible that you have power over that in the body? Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: This is not the framing of the question, but rather because the meaning of creating the body is nothing but its emergence from non-existence and its existence after it was not, and by that you describe yourselves in the action of accidents, how is it not permissible to describe [yourselves] with creating the body when there is no other? And there is no power except with God. So he answered with the action. And that is corrupt, because we have not realized for ourselves in our action the aspect which is the aspect of the body's existence and its being, so that would be incumbent upon us, but they have realized it, so it is incumbent upon them. And there is no power except with God. Then he said: Since the action of Zayd is nothing but an action by power, and you act by it as you did the action of Zayd? It was said: Because Zayd does not empower us to do his action, so we did not do it, but God has empowered you over the meaning by which the body existed, so what we confronted you with became incumbent upon you. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage contains a dialectical exchange. It begins with a statement about illnesses not being created, but then an objection is raised: if you (the Mu'tazila) claim that you have power to create accidents, why can't you create bodies? Maturidi responds by redefining the question: the essence of creating a body is just bringing it from non-existence, which is exactly what you do with accidents. So you should be able to describe yourselves as creating bodies. The opponent then answers with the notion of 'action' (al-fi‘l), but Maturidi critiques this: we do not have the specific aspect of a body's existence in our actions, so the argument does not apply to us, but it does apply to them. Next, the Zayd argument is presented: Zayd's action is by power, and you act by the same power. Why can't you do Zayd's action? The reply is that Zayd does not give us power over his specific action, but God gave you (Mu'tazila) power over the meaning that makes a body exist, so the objection stands against you.

Misconception: The objection is about physical creation versus supernatural creation.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that it is about the same process: bringing from non-existence into existence.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that creating a body is equivalent to creating an accident in terms of bringing from non-existence, and that the Mu'tazilite claim to create accidents does not grant them the power to create bodies without the specific aspect of bodily existence.

This lesson uses the Zayd argument to show that power over an action is only possible if Allah gives that power. The Proper Function of Intellect and Divine Attributes
Maturidi critiques intuition-based epistemology and defines intellect's role, leading to conclusion about divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of relying on mere intuition for knowledge, the true function of intellect in distinguishing and unifying by meanings, and the logical necessity that if God is described as knowing and powerful, He must truly possess knowledge and power.

Core Idea
Intellect is not for intuition but for reasoning through meanings to know God's attributes.

Maturidi begins by dismissing a flawed approach to knowledge—the idea that realities can be grasped through sheer intuition of intellects. He then clarifies the actual role of the intellect: it distinguishes between different things by their meanings and unifies similar things. Finally, he applies this reasoning to divine attributes, arguing that if we affirm knowledge and power of God, we must affirm them as real attributes. This passage establishes a foundational principle for rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the critique
Read the opening sentence: Maturidi calls a certain type of imagination baseless. Find what he is criticizing—the claim that realities are perceived by 'بداية العقول' (intuition of intellects).
This sets up his alternative view.
2
Understand intellect's function
Maturidi states that intellects are constituted to distinguish and unify based on meanings. Notice that he uses the terms 'مميزة' (distinguishing) and 'مؤلّفة' (unifying).
This is a definition of intellect's proper role.
3
Follow the logical implication
Maturidi argues that no one is known to be knowledgeable without knowledge, and lacking knowledge implies ignorance. Therefore, if we attribute knowledge and power to God, He must truly possess them.
This is key to affirming divine attributes as real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا النوع من الخيال الذي لا أصل ومتى تُدرك حقائق الأشياء ببداية العقول؟ وإنما العقول ركبت مميزة بين مختلف الأشياء بمعانيها التي توجب الاختلاف ومؤلّفة بين مجتمعها بمعان توجب الجمع، وذا حق الفكر والنظر ليوصل بهما إلى ذلك. ثم إنه لا أحد يُعْلَم عالما بشيء لا علم له به، قادرًا على شيء لا قدرة عليه؛ بل كل معروف بنفي القدرة والعلم موصوف بالجهل والعجز، فإذا احتمل الوصف بالقدرة والعلم فإنه عالم قادر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And this is a type of imagination that has no basis. And when are the realities of things perceived by the intuition of intellects? Rather, intellects are constituted distinguishing between different things by their meanings that necessitate difference, and unifying between similar things by meanings that necessitate combination, and this is the right of thought and reflection to arrive at that. Then, no one is known to be knowledgeable of something without knowledge, nor powerful over something without power; rather, everyone known by the negation of power and knowledge is described as ignorant and incapable. So if he admits the description of power and knowledge, then he is knowledgeable and powerful. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the opponent's claim that certain knowledge is based on 'بداية العقول' (intuition of intellects). Maturidi calls it baseless imagination. He asks rhetorically when realities are perceived by such intuition. Then he defines the proper function of intellects: they are structured to distinguish between different things based on their meanings, and to unify similar things based on shared meanings. This is the legitimate work of thought and reflection. Next, he states a logical principle: no one is known to be knowledgeable of something if he has no knowledge of it, nor powerful over something if he has no power over it. A person who lacks knowledge and power is described as ignorant and incapable. Therefore, if one attributes knowledge and power to God (as the opponent does implicitly or explicitly), then God must be truly Knowing and Powerful. This refutes any attempt to deny that God has these attributes in their real sense.

Key Terms
عقل
بداية العقول
معاني
علم
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That intuitive perception ('بداية العقول') is a valid source of knowledge of realities.
Correction: Maturidi argues it is baseless imagination; true knowledge requires reasoning through meanings.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are intellects constituted to do?
A) To perceive things directly without reasoning
B) To distinguish between different things by their meanings and unify similar things
C) To invent arbitrary distinctions
D) To rely on sense perception alone

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that true knowledge comes from reasoning through meanings, not intuition, and that affirming divine attributes logically implies they are real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever described Allah as 'All-Knowing' or 'All-Powerful' without truly believing that these attributes are real in His essence? If so, how can you correct your heart to affirm His attributes with certainty, as Maturidi argues? Will you commit today to saying His names with deeper conviction?
?
How often do you rely on your intuition or gut feeling about religious matters instead of thinking through what the intellect and revelation teach? Where in your life have you stopped reasoning and started assuming? Will you choose to engage in reflective thought (الفكر والنظر) daily, starting now?
?
This lesson reveals that intellect is for distinguishing and unifying through meanings. When you hear a new claim about Allah, do you examine its meaning carefully, or do you accept or reject it emotionally? Will you train yourself to hold every belief about Allah to the standard of sound reasoning, as Maturidi shows?

Maturidi on Intuition and the Reality of Evil
Maturidi refutes the claim that evil is known by intuition and affirms that God created examples of ugliness for warning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the claim that evil is known by 'intuition of reason' and how he affirms that God creates examples of ugliness as warnings.

Core Idea
What seems like intuition is just natural aversion; God's creation includes real ugliness as a warning.

Maturidi engages with an opponent who asserts that evil is self-evident through rational intuition. In this passage, he carefully deconstructs that claim, arguing that what the opponent calls intuition is actually a natural aversion. Moreover, he points out that God's creation does contain truly ugly things, but these serve as warnings, not as evidence against God's wisdom. This lesson unpacks Maturidi's nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice the opponent's claim and Maturidi's response.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question at the end.
2
Analyze Maturidi's argument
Identify the two parts: first, the dismissal of intuition as mere natural aversion; second, the affirmation that God creates ugly things as warnings. See how he uses the opponent's own denial of ugliness against him.
The argument hinges on the distinction between 'بديهة العقل' and 'طباع'.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Maturidi emphasizes that God creates ugly examples as warnings. How does this defend divine wisdom?
This connects to broader themes of good and evil in Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن رجع إلى اعتبار المعاني التي هي أسباب حقائق الأشياء فذلك له مسلم، ولا معنى لقوله ببديهة العقل إنما ذلك حق الطباع ونفارِه. ثم يُنكر أن يكون في خلق الله قبيحا في الحقيقة وشراً وفسادا، على وجود مالا يحصى من ذلك على هذه الأوصاف بالعقول. بل الله جل ثناؤه بما جعلها كذلك ضرب بها مثل ما قبح من الأفعال وأراد تفظيع منظره الذي أُوعِدَ به ذو عقل؛ أين الذي يُنكر مثل هذا من دعوى بداية العقول؟
And if he returns to considering the meanings that are causes of the realities of things, then that is granted to him. And there is no meaning to his statement 'by the intuition of reason'; rather, that is the right of natures and their aversion. Then he denies that there is in the creation of God anything that is truly ugly, evil, or corruption, despite the existence of countless things with these descriptions according to intellects. Rather, Allah—may His praise be exalted—by making them thus, struck with them an example of ugly actions and intended to make horrific the scene that a person of intellect is warned of. Where is the one who denies such a thing from the claim of the intuition of intellects?
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage addresses an opponent who claims that certain actions are known to be evil by 'بديهة العقل' (intuition of reason). Maturidi first concedes that if the opponent means the essential meanings that cause things to be what they are, that is acceptable. But when he invokes 'بديهة العقل', that is meaningless; what he really refers to is 'حق الطباع ونفارها' (the natural disposition and its aversion) – a gut feeling, not intellectual intuition. Then Maturidi points out a contradiction: the opponent denies that there is anything truly ugly, evil, or corrupt in God's creation, yet intellects recognize countless things as such. Moreover, God Himself has described certain actions as ugly and has used them as examples to warn people. So how can the opponent claim that such ugliness is not real? The rhetorical question 'أين الذي يُنكر مثل هذا من دعوى بداية العقول؟' challenges the opponent: where is the one who denies this, based on the claim of 'بداية العقول'?

Key Terms
معاني
بديهة العقل
طباع
قبيح
شر
فساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil actions are known to be evil by an 'intuition of reason' (بديهة العقل) that provides objective moral knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi argues that what is called 'intuition' is actually a natural aversion (حق الطباع ونفارها), not a rational insight. Moreover, God's creation does include truly ugly things, but they serve as warnings, contradicting the claim that nothing in creation is truly ugly.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is the real source of the opponent's claimed 'intuition of reason' regarding evil?
A) Divine revelation
B) Natural disposition and its aversion
C) Logical deduction
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Maturidi denies that evil is known by rational intuition and instead sees it as natural aversion, while affirming that God creates examples of ugliness as warnings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a strong revulsion toward something, how often do you stop to ask whether it is reasoned judgment or just instinct? Will you now commit to examining your reactions before acting on them?
?
Maturidi says God created ugly things as warnings. Look at your own life—what ‘ugly’ situation are you currently in that might be a divine warning? How will you respond with reflection instead of denial?
?
The opponent denied that real evil exists in creation. Have you ever found yourself dismissing evil as just a human perspective, avoiding its weight as a test? What step will you take today to recognize evil as real and turn it into a means of drawing closer to God?
