Defining Evil and Dismissing a Simplistic Answer
Maturidi defines evil and dismisses an opponent's easy answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's threefold definition of evil (qubh) in human actions and see why he dismisses the opponent's easy answer as insufficient.

Core Idea
Evil is defined by consequence, not mere human evaluation.

Maturidi begins his refutation by clarifying what constitutes evil in human actions. He identifies three specific types of evil, then quickly dismisses the opponent's facile response. This sets the stage for a deeper theological discussion on divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three categories of evil listed at the start.
The passage includes a footnote marker (6) which is part of the original text.
2
Identify the three categories of evil
Find the three types of evil Maturidi enumerates: harm in the consequence, ingratitude for blessing, and opposing the Lord. Understand each category.
These categories are central to Maturidi's ethical framework.
3
Analyze the dismissal of the opponent's answer
Notice how Maturidi calls the opponent's answer 'easy' and does not give it further weight. Reflect on why he might do this.
The opponent's answer is likely a Mu'tazilite argument that Maturidi considers flawed.
4
Connect to the next lesson
Consider how this definition sets up the need for a more detailed refutation of the master-slave analogy in the following passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس كذلك ، وهو ضرر العاقبة أو كفران النعمة أو مخالفة الرب في الفعل. وإذا كان به دفعه، ولزمه السؤال " ، ولم يُلْزِم خصمه فيها عارضه بالزمن الذي أجابه بما ينقض عليه ليعلم به بعده عن الحق، فيما يوافق عليه ويخالف فيه جميعا . ثم إن جواب خصمه سهل، وهو والله أعلم.
٦
.
ما بينا .
ثم قال : ذلك الذي قيل فيمن يفعل الحاجة قيل . والأول أيضاً قبح ممن لا يملك التقوية لو طلب منه وتُضرع إليه . ثم عارض نفسه بمن يدفع إلى عبده ما يعلم أنه يعصيه به؛ فقال: قد يكون ذلك حكمة، نحو من يعلم بخبر الرسول أنه لا يؤمن يجوز أن يطعمه، ونحو ذلك مما يعلم من تأمله جهله بما عارض به نفسه، لأن الذي ذكره لا يجوز أن يعطيه ليؤمن بذلك بعد علمه بأنه لا يفعل، وإنما يعطيه لمنافع سوى هذا. والمعتزلة تزعم أنه أعطى القوة ليؤمن بها، وهو يعلم أنه يكفر بها، فليس ذلك مما قدر في شيء . ثم المعارضة كانت فيمن يعصيه، فلا أحد يعد نفسه في الحكماء إذا علم أن عبده بالذي يعطيه يعصيه ولا يكتسب رضاه بل يعمل بعدواته وشتمه ولا قوة إلا بالله . ولكن ذا عندنا إنما قبح في الشاهد لأن ذلك يضره ويدخل عليه الألم، وذلك لا يحتمل أمر الغائب. والله الموفق. ثم قال: «لأنـ [ه] ليس لمن حضر أن يمتحن" ونحو ذلك؛ فأنى له هذا بعد تقديره فعل الغائب بالشاهد على تحقيق ما يجد فيه لأنه يقابل بجميع ما أنكر وادعى الخروج من الحكمة. إن ذلك في فعل من ذلك وصفه ، فأما الله سبحانه " فهو متعال عن وقوف عقل مثله على حقيقة ذلك . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
It is not so, and it is harm of the consequence, or ingratitude for the blessing, or opposing the Lord in action. And if it is through him repelling it, and the question is incumbent upon him, and he did not compel his opponent in it (that) he objected to him with the time in which he answered him with what nullifies it so that he may know by it his distance from the truth, in what he agrees upon and disagrees completely. Then the answer of his opponent is easy, and it is with God — and God knows best. 6. What we explained. Then he said: That which was said concerning one who does a need (is) said. And the first also is ugly (qabīḥ) from one who does not possess giving strength if it is asked of him and supplicated to him. Then he opposed himself with one who gives to his slave what he knows that he disobeys him with; so he said: That may be wisdom, like one who knows by the news of the messenger that he will not believe, it is permissible for him to feed him, and the like of that which one who reflects on it knows his ignorance of what he opposed himself with, because what he mentioned is not permissible for him to give him to believe by that after his knowledge that he will not do, but rather he gives him for benefits other than this. And the Mu'tazila claim that He gave the power so that he may believe by it, while He knows that he will disbelieve by it, so that is not something that is determined in anything. Then the opposition was concerning one who disobeys Him, so no one counts himself among the wise if he knows that his slave, by what he gives him, disobeys him and does not earn his pleasure but rather works with enmity and insult—and there is no power except with God. But this, in our view, is only ugly (qabīḥ) in the witnessed (shāhid) because that harms him and brings pain upon him, and that does not apply to the matter of the unseen (ghā'ib). And God is the Grantor of success. Then he said: 'Because it is not for one who is present to test' and the like; so how can he have this after his equating the action of the unseen (ghā'ib) with the witnessed (shāhid) upon the verification of what he finds in it, because he is confronted with all that he denied and claimed to exit from wisdom? That is in the action of one whose description is that. As for God Almighty, He is exalted above the knowledge of a mind like his (the opponent's) regarding the reality of that. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument (likely a Mu'tazilite, probably al-Ka'bi) using a specific analogy about a master giving to a slave to illustrate the problem of divine action and human evil. Maturidi begins by stating that the evil (qubh) under discussion is either harm in the consequence, ingratitude for blessing, or disobedience to the Lord. He then notes that the opponent's answer is easy to dismiss. The opponent presented a case of someone doing a needful act, but Maturidi counters that such an act is only ugly from a human who lacks power to strengthen. Then the opponent compares God to a master who gives his slave what he knows will cause disobedience. Maturidi responds that such a gift may be wisdom if given for other benefits, not for the purpose of inciting disbelief. The Mu'tazila claim that God gave power for belief despite foreknowledge of disbelief, which is not sound. In the witnessed realm (i.e., human-to-human), giving a slave a tool for rebellion is considered ugly because it harms the giver. But this analogy does not apply to the unseen divine realm (al-ghā'ib), because God is not subject to harm or pain. The argument that one cannot test another is also dismissed; Maturidi emphasizes that human reasoning cannot fully comprehend divine wisdom. The passage concludes that God is transcendent above such anthropomorphic comparisons.

Key Terms
قبح
ضرر العاقبة
كفران النعمة
مخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent might think that evil is simply any act that leads to punishment or is arbitrarily labeled as bad.
Correction: Maturidi specifies three concrete categories of evil, showing that it is defined by specific consequences and relational violations, not mere labeling.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is NOT one of the categories of evil?
A) Harm of the consequence
B) Ingratitude for blessing
C) Disobedience to the Lord
D) Causing pain to others

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defines evil in human actions as harm, ingratitude, or disobedience, and dismisses any simplistic answer as inadequate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you minimized the harm of your actions by focusing on your own intentions, ignoring the actual consequences? Can you bring a specific example to mind right now and repent for it?
?
Think of one major blessing from God that you have taken for granted or used in disobedience. How does Maturidi's category of 'ingratitude for the blessing' reframe how you see that sin? What concrete step will you take today to show gratitude?
?
Is there an area of your life where you knowingly oppose the Lord in action—perhaps a small habit you justify? How would your schedule change if you treated that as a genuine instance of 'mukhālafat al-rabb' and not just a minor slip?

The Master-Slave Analogy and the Mu'tazilite Claim
Maturidi refutes the master-slave analogy and the Mu'tazilite claim about divine giving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the master-slave analogy and why the Mu'tazilite claim that God gives power for belief despite foreknowledge of disbelief is flawed.

Core Idea
Giving a tool for rebellion is wisdom or folly depending on the giver's attributes.

Maturidi now engages directly with the Mu'tazilite analogy of a master giving a slave a tool that leads to disobedience. He shows that such an act can be wisdom in certain contexts, but the Mu'tazila misapply it to God, ignoring the distinction between human and divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the analogy
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the master-gives-slave analogy and note how Maturidi initially responds that it might be wisdom.
The passage includes a self-objection by Maturidi.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Find where Maturidi states the Mu'tazila claim: that God gives power for belief while knowing the person will disbelieve. Note his rejection.
This is the core of the rational objection from Mu'tazila.
3
Analyze Maturidi's response
See how Maturidi argues that giving a slave a tool for rebellion is only ugly in human interactions because it harms the giver, but this does not apply to God.
This introduces the shahid/gha'ib distinction.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite claim that Maturidi refutes in this passage?
A) That God gives power for belief while knowing the person will disbelieve
B) That God does not know the future
C) That humans have free will

Key Takeaway
The master-slave analogy fails because it relies on human limitations that do not apply to God, especially the experience of harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been given abilities that you sometimes use to disobey God. If God knows this and still gives you strength, what does that demand from you in return? Are you using His gifts for the benefits He intended, or are you ignoring His wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought it unjust for God to give power for disbelief. But Maturidi says God gives for other benefits, even if you misuse it. When you receive a blessing and then sin with it, do you consider that God still had a wise purpose? How does that change your view of His mercy and your own responsibility?
?
If a human master would be harmed by your rebellion, but God is not, does that make your disobedience less serious or more? How does knowing that God does not suffer from your sins affect your urgency to repent and change?

Divine Transcendence Beyond Human Analogy
Maturidi concludes by asserting divine transcendence beyond human analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's conclusion that God's wisdom is beyond human comprehension and that comparing the unseen to the witnessed is invalid.

Core Idea
God's actions are beyond human analogy.

Maturidi concludes his refutation by emphasizing that equating the unseen divine realm with the witnessed human realm is a fundamental error. He asserts that God's wisdom is transcendent and cannot be fully grasped by human minds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key claim
Read the final part of the refutation. Notice Maturidi's statement that equating the unseen with the witnessed is erroneous.
This is the culmination of his argument.
2
Understand the limitation of human reasoning
Maturidi says that God is exalted above the knowledge of a mind like the opponent's. Reflect on what this implies for theological reasoning.
This emphasizes divine transcendence (tanzih).
3
Connect to Islamic theology
Consider how this principle of tanzih protects God from anthropomorphism and preserves His uniqueness.
This is a key Maturidi principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is not so, and it is harm of the consequence, or ingratitude for the blessing, or opposing the Lord in action. And if it is through him repelling it, and the question is incumbent upon him, and he did not compel his opponent in it (that) he objected to him with the time in which he answered him with what nullifies it so that he may know by it his distance from the truth, in what he agrees upon and disagrees completely. Then the answer of his opponent is easy, and it is with God — and God knows best. 6. What we explained. Then he said: That which was said concerning one who does a need (is) said. And the first also is ugly (qabīḥ) from one who does not possess giving strength if it is asked of him and supplicated to him. Then he opposed himself with one who gives to his slave what he knows that he disobeys him with; so he said: That may be wisdom, like one who knows by the news of the messenger that he will not believe, it is permissible for him to feed him, and the like of that which one who reflects on it knows his ignorance of what he opposed himself with, because what he mentioned is not permissible for him to give him to believe by that after his knowledge that he will not do, but rather he gives him for benefits other than this. And the Mu'tazila claim that He gave the power so that he may believe by it, while He knows that he will disbelieve by it, so that is not something that is determined in anything. Then the opposition was concerning one who disobeys Him, so no one counts himself among the wise if he knows that his slave, by what he gives him, disobeys him and does not earn his pleasure but rather works with enmity and insult—and there is no power except with God. But this, in our view, is only ugly (qabīḥ) in the witnessed (shāhid) because that harms him and brings pain upon him, and that does not apply to the matter of the unseen (ghā'ib). And God is the Grantor of success. Then he said: 'Because it is not for one who is present to test' and the like; so how can he have this after his equating the action of the unseen (ghā'ib) with the witnessed (shāhid) upon the verification of what he finds in it, because he is confronted with all that he denied and claimed to exit from wisdom? That is in the action of one whose description is that. As for God Almighty, He is exalted above the knowledge of a mind like his (the opponent's) regarding the reality of that. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument (likely a Mu'tazilite, probably al-Ka'bi) using a specific analogy about a master giving to a slave to illustrate the problem of divine action and human evil. Maturidi begins by stating that the evil (qubh) under discussion is either harm in the consequence, ingratitude for blessing, or disobedience to the Lord. He then notes that the opponent's answer is easy to dismiss. The opponent presented a case of someone doing a needful act, but Maturidi counters that such an act is only ugly from a human who lacks power to strengthen. Then the opponent compares God to a master who gives his slave what he knows will cause disobedience. Maturidi responds that such a gift may be wisdom if given for other benefits, not for the purpose of inciting disbelief. The Mu'tazila claim that God gave power for belief despite foreknowledge of disbelief, which is not sound. In the witnessed realm (i.e., human-to-human), giving a slave a tool for rebellion is considered ugly because it harms the giver. But this analogy does not apply to the unseen divine realm (al-ghā'ib), because God is not subject to harm or pain. The argument that one cannot test another is also dismissed; Maturidi emphasizes that human reasoning cannot fully comprehend divine wisdom. The passage concludes that God is transcendent above such anthropomorphic comparisons.

شاهد

متعال

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent thinks that human reasoning can fully understand divine actions and judge them by human standards.
Correction: Maturidi asserts that God is exalted above such comprehension; equating the unseen with the witnessed is a category error.

A) God's wisdom can be understood by the opponent
B) God is exalted above the knowledge of such a mind
C) God's actions are similar to human actions
D) The opponent is correct

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom transcends human analogy; equating the unseen with the witnessed is a fundamental error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you face a situation that seems 'unwise' or unjust according to your reasoning, do you truly submit to God's transcendence, or do you hold back your heart in doubt? What concrete step can you take today to trust His wisdom above your own?
?
The opponent in this passage is accused of 'equating the unseen with the witnessed.' In what subtle ways do you do the same with your own thoughts about God? How can you cultivate awe (taqwa) that silences your comparison?

The Burden Within Capacity and Divine Foreknowledge
Maturidi presents a dialectical argument on divine foreknowledge and human burden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the interpretation of Q2:286 to argue against the view that divine foreknowledge negates human responsibility, through a dialectical analysis of three possible scenarios.

Core Idea
Foreknowledge does not remove capacity; the burden remains within our scope.

Maturidi begins by referencing his previous interpretation of the Quranic verse 'No burden beyond capacity' and the ugliness of imposing a burden. He then launches into a hypothetical argument against an opponent who claims that divine foreknowledge or future will somehow nullifies the burden. This passage forces the opponent to confront logical consequences of their own positions, showing that the burden remains valid and the capacity intact.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Quranic foundation
Notice that Maturidi builds on a prior interpretation of Q2:286. This verse is the anchor for the whole discussion about burden and capacity.
He says 'we have explained' – he is not starting from scratch.
2
Identify the opponent's starting claim
Look for the phrase 'على أنه يقال' – here Maturidi sets up a conjecture: what if God knows someone won't act, or that they will will an act later? This is the trigger for the dialectic.
The opponent likely believes that such foreknowledge undermines the burden.
3
Follow the three scenarios
Maturidi presents three possible replies from the opponent: (1) the person does the opposite, (2) is prevented, (3) the burden is lifted. Each leads to a contradiction or unacceptable consequence.
Watch how each reply nullifies a key tenet of the opponent's own system.
4
Understand the 'necessitating will' refutation
Maturidi argues that if the opponent says the act's opposite is done, that disproves their concept of 'necessitating will' (al-irāda al-mūjiba), because command and ability coexist with the act, not before it.
The opponent believed will necessitates action immediately.
5
Grasp the climax on the burden being lifted
If the opponent says the burden is lifted, then no one foreknown to disobey would be subject to divine trial (miḥna) – but that contradicts the very purpose of revelation and command.
Maturidi calls this the utmost limit of ugliness (qubh) in the opponent's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وقد بينا تأويل قوله : لا يكلف الله نفسا إلا وسعها ﴾ [البقرة، ٢٨٦/٢]، وبينا قبح المكلّف
٤
١٤١ ] قوله في إسقاط التكليف وقت الفعل وإبطال القدرة عليه فيصير في التحصيل هو على غير الوسع . على أنه يقال : كيف لو كان في علم الله أنه لا يفعل، أو في علمه أنه يريد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه ؟ ومن قولكم: إن من أراد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه [ف]إنه يفعله لا محالة، إلا أن يُمنع أن يفعله؛ أيُمنع أو يفعل ضده؟ فإن قال: يفعل ضده، أبطل قوله في كون الإرادة قبل الفعل وألزم نفسه الأمر معه والقدرة معه وبطل قولهم في الإرادة الموجبة. وإن قال : يُمنع، فقد ألزم من في علم الله أنه لا يفعل التكليف بقوة تُمنع عن الفعل، وهو في التحقيق تكليف العاجز الممنوع. وإن قال " : ترتفع الكلفة ، أبطل أن يكون أحد ـ ممن في علم الله أنه لا يطيعه - مما تضمنته المحنة ولزمه الأمر والنهي، وذلك غاية ما ينتهي إليه القول في القبح. وعلى ذلك أمر الإرادة، إن الله إذ يمنعه عن الفعل الذي في علمه أنه لا يفعله لا بد أن يمنعه عن الإرادة ، وفي ذلك منع عن الطاعة عنده والخير. ثم يقال: المروي عن الذي روى أنه سل سيفه على رسول الله فمنعه الله بقبض يده " ، أكان ذلك المنع أصلح له في الدين وأخير له أو الإطلاق؟ فإن قال: الإطلاق، فقد أقر بأن الله قد يفعل بعباده ما كان غيره أصلح لهم] في الدين؛ وإن قال: المنع، فقد أقر أن المنع قد يكون أصلح؛ فكل عاص لم يمنع عنه لم يفعل به الأصلح. ولا قوة إلا بالله. واحتجاجه بقوله : ولو استطعنا لخرجنا معكم ﴾ [التوبة، ٤٢/٩]، قد بينا ما عليه في ذلك،
ه
وما يُظهر أن خصمه أشد لاتباع ذلك ومعرفته منه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم عارض بما لا يُحقق لهم " العذر بالفقر إلا بما لا يتهيأ لهم الفعل، وذلك المعنى في فقد القدرة موجود.
[Initial 'ل' possibly stray] And we have explained the interpretation of His saying: 'God does not burden any soul beyond its capacity' [Q2:286], and we explained the ugliness (qubh) of the one burdened (al-mukallaf) [footnote] his saying regarding the removal of the burden (taklīf) at the time of the act and the nullification of the ability (qudra) over it, so it becomes in accomplishment upon other than capacity. However, it is said: How would it be if it is in God's knowledge that he will not do, or in His knowledge that he wills the act in the time following it? And from your statement: that one who wills the act in the time following it, then he will do it inevitably, unless he is prevented from doing it; is he prevented or does he do its opposite? If he says: he does its opposite, he nullifies his statement regarding the existence of the will before the act and imposes upon himself the command with it and the ability with it, and their statement about the necessitating will (al-irāda al-mūjiba) becomes invalid. And if he says: he is prevented, then he imposes upon one who is in God's knowledge that he will not do the burden (taklīf) with a power that is prevented from the act, and in reality it is a burden upon the incapable, the prevented. And if he says: the burden is lifted, he nullifies that anyone—from among those in God's knowledge that he will not obey Him—is included in the trial (miḥna) and subject to command and prohibition, and that is the utmost that the statement regarding ugliness (qubh) reaches. And upon that is the matter of the will (irāda): when God prevents him from the act which is in His knowledge that he will not do, He must prevent him from the will, and in that is prevention from obedience and good with him. Then it is said: The narrated account concerning the one who drew his sword against the Messenger of God, so God prevented him by seizing his hand—was that prevention better for him in religion and more good for him, or the release? If he says: the release, then he acknowledges that God may do with His servants what other than Him is better for them in religion; and if he says: the prevention, then he acknowledges that prevention may be better; so every disobedient who is not prevented, the better is not done for him. And there is no power except with God. And his argument with His saying: 'If we had been able, we would have gone out with you' [Q9:42]—we have explained what is upon that, and what shows that his opponent is more intense in following that and knowing it than him. And there is no power except with God. Then he objected with what does not realize for them the excuse of poverty except by what does not enable them the action, and that meaning is present in the loss of ability.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is an exposition and argument about the interpretation of Quran 2:286 ('God does not burden any soul beyond its capacity') and its implications for divine foreknowledge, human will, and the problem of evil. Maturidi states that they have already explained the interpretation of this verse and the ugliness (qubh) of the one who is burdened (al-mukallaf), referring to previous discussion. He then presents a hypothetical: what if it is in God's knowledge that a person will not perform an act, or that he will will the act only at a later time? He draws from the opponent's own position that one who wills an act at a future time will inevitably do it unless prevented. The opponent is then faced with options: if the act's opposite is done, that nullifies the claim that will precedes the act and necessitates command and ability at the same time, invalidating the notion of a necessitating will (al-irāda al-mūjiba). If the act is prevented, then the burden is on the incapable prevented person. If the opponent says the burden is lifted, then no one foreknown to disobey would be subject to trial, which Maturidi considers the logical conclusion of the opponent's position. Maturidi then discusses the will: if God prevents an act, He must also prevent the will, which then prevents obedience and good. He then cites a story about a man who drew his sword on the Prophet and was prevented by God (seizing his hand). The question is whether this prevention was better for the man's religion or if release was better. If the opponent says release was better, he admits God does not always do what is most beneficial; if prevention is better, then every disobedient who is not prevented has not been treated with the best. Maturidi notes that the opponent's argument from Q9:42 has been addressed, and that the opponent is actually more eager to follow this reasoning. The passage ends with an objection that the excuse of poverty (inability) is not realized except by actual lack of capacity, and that meaning is present in the loss of ability.

Key Terms
تكليف
وسع
قدرة
إرادة موجبة
محنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God knows someone will not do an act, then the person cannot be held responsible or burdened.
Correction: Maturidi shows that foreknowledge does not remove capacity; the burden remains valid, and the opponent's own logic leads to absurdities like no trial for the disobedient.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens if the opponent says the burden is lifted for those foreknown to disobey?
A) They become free from sin
B) No one would be subject to trial or command
C) They lose all ability
D) They become believers

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that divine foreknowledge does not remove human capacity; the burden remains within capacity, and any attempt to argue otherwise leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used Allah's knowledge of your future actions as an excuse for sin, thinking 'He already knows I'll fail'? How does Maturidi's argument confront that excuse and call you to take full responsibility for your choices?
?
When you face a command that feels difficult, do you secretly question whether Allah has truly made it within your capacity? What would it mean for you to honestly trust that no burden is beyond your scope, even when you feel weak?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's foreknowledge does not diminish your ability to obey, what one area of your life would you stop making excuses in—and what concrete step will you take today to act on that belief?

Prevention, Will, and the Problem of Evil
Maturidi discusses the relationship between prevention, will, and the problem of evil using a historical example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument about prevention and will, including how the story of the man who drew his sword against the Prophet exposes the opponent's inconsistency regarding what is best for a person in religion.

Core Idea
Prevention of sin can be better than freedom, but not being prevented implies God does not always choose the best.

Having refuted the opponent's initial positions, Maturidi now turns to the nature of will and prevention. He argues that if God prevents an act, He must also prevent the will, which blocks obedience and good. He then invokes a narrated incident where God prevented a man from harming the Prophet by seizing his hand. This raises a dilemma: was the prevention better for that man's religion or was freedom better? The opponent's answer will either admit that God can do what is less than optimal (a form of evil) or affirm that prevention is sometimes better, thereby condemning all sinners who were not prevented.

Guided Path
1
Follow the argument about will and prevention
Maturidi connects prevention of an act to prevention of the will. Understand that if God prevents the act, He must also prevent the will, and this prevents obedience and good.
This counters the opponent's idea that will is independent.
2
Analyze the story of the sword-wielding man
Read the narrative about the man who drew his sword on the Prophet. The Prophet was saved by God causing the man's hand to freeze. The question: was this prevention better or worse for the man's religion?
The story is used as a test case for divine prevention.
3
Explore the two possible answers
If the opponent says release (no prevention) was better, they admit God sometimes does not do what is most beneficial. If they say prevention was better, then every sinner not prevented has not received the best treatment from God.
Either answer creates a problem for the opponent's theology.
4
Connect to the broader issue of divine justice
Maturidi uses this to challenge any claim that God always does what is optimally beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). The opponent is forced to choose between two undesirable conclusions.
This is a classic argument in theodicy.
5
Note the reference to Q9:42 and the poverty excuse
Maturidi mentions that the opponent's argument from Q9:42 (hypocrites' excuse of poverty) has been refuted elsewhere, and that the true excuse is inability, which is the loss of capacity – a different matter.
He closes by reasserting that the issue is about actual power, not foreknowledge.

وما يُظهر أن خصمه أشد لاتباع ذلك ومعرفته منه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم عارض بما لا يُحقق لهم " العذر بالفقر إلا بما لا يتهيأ لهم الفعل، وذلك المعنى في فقد القدرة موجود.
[Initial 'ل' possibly stray] And we have explained the interpretation of His saying: 'God does not burden any soul beyond its capacity' [Q2:286], and we explained the ugliness (qubh) of the one burdened (al-mukallaf) [footnote] his saying regarding the removal of the burden (taklīf) at the time of the act and the nullification of the ability (qudra) over it, so it becomes in accomplishment upon other than capacity. However, it is said: How would it be if it is in God's knowledge that he will not do, or in His knowledge that he wills the act in the time following it? And from your statement: that one who wills the act in the time following it, then he will do it inevitably, unless he is prevented from doing it; is he prevented or does he do its opposite? If he says: he does its opposite, he nullifies his statement regarding the existence of the will before the act and imposes upon himself the command with it and the ability with it, and their statement about the necessitating will (al-irāda al-mūjiba) becomes invalid. And if he says: he is prevented, then he imposes upon one who is in God's knowledge that he will not do the burden (taklīf) with a power that is prevented from the act, and in reality it is a burden upon the incapable, the prevented. And if he says: the burden is lifted, he nullifies that anyone—from among those in God's knowledge that he will not obey Him—is included in the trial (miḥna) and subject to command and prohibition, and that is the utmost that the statement regarding ugliness (qubh) reaches. And upon that is the matter of the will (irāda): when God prevents him from the act which is in His knowledge that he will not do, He must prevent him from the will, and in that is prevention from obedience and good with him. Then it is said: The narrated account concerning the one who drew his sword against the Messenger of God, so God prevented him by seizing his hand—was that prevention better for him in religion and more good for him, or the release? If he says: the release, then he acknowledges that God may do with His servants what other than Him is better for them in religion; and if he says: the prevention, then he acknowledges that prevention may be better; so every disobedient who is not prevented, the better is not done for him. And there is no power except with God. And his argument with His saying: 'If we had been able, we would have gone out with you' [Q9:42]—we have explained what is upon that, and what shows that his opponent is more intense in following that and knowing it than him. And there is no power except with God. Then he objected with what does not realize for them the excuse of poverty except by what does not enable them the action, and that meaning is present in the loss of ability.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is an exposition and argument about the interpretation of Quran 2:286 ('God does not burden any soul beyond its capacity') and its implications for divine foreknowledge, human will, and the problem of evil. Maturidi states that they have already explained the interpretation of this verse and the ugliness (qubh) of the one who is burdened (al-mukallaf), referring to previous discussion. He then presents a hypothetical: what if it is in God's knowledge that a person will not perform an act, or that he will will the act only at a later time? He draws from the opponent's own position that one who wills an act at a future time will inevitably do it unless prevented. The opponent is then faced with options: if the act's opposite is done, that nullifies the claim that will precedes the act and necessitates command and ability at the same time, invalidating the notion of a necessitating will (al-irāda al-mūjiba). If the act is prevented, then the burden is on the incapable prevented person. If the opponent says the burden is lifted, then no one foreknown to disobey would be subject to trial, which Maturidi considers the logical conclusion of the opponent's position. Maturidi then discusses the will: if God prevents an act, He must also prevent the will, which then prevents obedience and good. He then cites a story about a man who drew his sword on the Prophet and was prevented by God (seizing his hand). The question is whether this prevention was better for the man's religion or if release was better. If the opponent says release was better, he admits God does not always do what is most beneficial; if prevention is better, then every disobedient who is not prevented has not been treated with the best. Maturidi notes that the opponent's argument from Q9:42 has been addressed, and that the opponent is actually more eager to follow this reasoning. The passage ends with an objection that the excuse of poverty (inability) is not realized except by actual lack of capacity, and that meaning is present in the loss of ability.

Key Terms
إرادة
منع
إرادة موجبة
أصلح
محنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God prevents a sin, it is always better not to have prevented it because it interferes with free will.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the opponent is forced to admit either that God sometimes does less than the best (if prevention was not better) or that not preventing sinners is a failure to do what is best for them.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude if the opponent says that prevention (the seizing of the hand) was better for the man's religion?
A) God always does what is best
B) Every sinner not prevented has not received what is best from God
C) The man would have become a better Muslim if not prevented
D) The opponent admits God can be unjust

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the story of the sword-wielding man to force the opponent to choose between two theologically problematic positions: either God sometimes does less than the best, or not preventing sinners is a failure to do best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own sins, do you ever feel that God should have prevented you? What does that feeling reveal about your reliance on God's will versus your own? Are you willing to accept that His non-prevention may be part of a greater wisdom that you cannot grasp?
?
The man who drew his sword against the Prophet was physically stopped by a miracle. When you are about to commit a sin, what 'prevents' you? Is it your own will, fear of consequences, or a conscious turning to God? Honestly, do you thank God for the times He prevented you, or do you take credit for your restraint?
?
If prevention is truly better, then why does God not prevent all sin? Does this challenge your understanding of His justice and wisdom? How do you reconcile the fact that He allows you to choose sin, even when prevention might be better for you?

Divine Power and Action in Three Temporal States
Maturidi establishes that divine power sees actions in three temporal states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's doctrine that divine power coexists with an action only at the moment of its existence, not before or after.

Core Idea
Divine power is seen only with the action, not apart from it.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's *Kitab al-Tawhid* clarifies how divine power relates to actions through three temporal states. Using the analogy of seeing a thrown object, Maturidi argues that power is not a separate entity existing prior to an action but is seen by God only when the action exists. He then summarizes the three states—non-existence before, existence during, and annihilation after—and concludes that power is seen only in the state of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument and rhythm come through before interpreting.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the three temporal states of action
Find where Maturidi lists the three states: non-existence (before), annihilation (after), and existence (during). Notice how he applies this to power.
These states are key to understanding the relationship.
3
Understand the analogy of throwing
Maturidi uses the example of seeing a thrown object and its removal. Reflect on how seeing the action and its causes together does not negate the action's validity, and how this applies to power.
The analogy illustrates simultaneous presence.
4
Examine the response to the opponent's proof
The passage addresses an objection based on Qur'an 2:282 about inability to dictate. Understand Maturidi's interpretation: the verse refers to inability due to lack of knowledge or incapacity (istita'at al-'ajz).
This shows how Maturidi handles scriptural evidence.
5
Reflect on the conclusion: 'La quwwata illa billah'
The passage ends with the affirmation that there is no power except by God. Consider how this reinforces the idea that power is not independent but always with the action and from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يراه يرى القدرة معه ؛ على ما يرى إلقاء الشيء وإخراجه مع خروج ذلك وإلقائه ، ولم يبطل حق الإلقاء والإخراج بما يرى الشيء على ما يراه فمثله الذي ذكرت. وإذ جاز أن يراه موجودًا ومعه الأسباب كلها، ولم يبعد ذلك، فمثله القوة، بل كذلك يجب أن يرى القدرة، كما كذلك يجب أن يرى الفعل مع الأسباب.
وجملته أن للفعل وقت العدم وهو قبله، ووقت الفناء وهو بعده، ووقت الوجود وهو في حاله، ولا محالة يراه الله مع أحواله على ما ذكر لا غير؛ وكذلك الأوقات التي تقع فيها الأفعال والأمكنة، فعلى ذلك الأسباب، فمثله القوة يراها معدومة قبله، فانية بعده، موجودة معه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . واحتجاجه بقوله: «أو لا يستطيع أن يُمل هو » ، فجوابه كما سلف بيانه، مع احتماله لمعنى لا يُحسن، وهو استطاعة العجز أيضا. دليل ذلك ما بينا أن قدرة التمام لا تكون قبل الابتداء، وقد أضيف إليه الكل. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
He sees power with it; just as he sees the throwing of a thing and its removal with the exit of that and its throwing, and it did not invalidate the right of throwing and removal by what he sees the thing as he sees it, so the like of that which I mentioned. And when it is permissible that he sees it existing and with it all causes, and that is not improbable, so the like of that is power, rather thus it must be that he sees power, just as thus it must be that he sees action with the causes. And its summary is that action has a time of non-existence which is before it, and a time of annihilation which is after it, and a time of existence which is at its moment, and inevitably God sees it with its states as mentioned, not otherwise; and similarly the times in which actions and places occur, so likewise the causes, so the like of that is power: he sees it non-existent before it, annihilated after it, existing with it. And there is no power except by God. And his argument using His saying: 'Or is he not able to dictate he?' then its answer is as previously explained, with its possibility meaning 'does not know', and it is also the ability of incapacity. The proof of that is what we have shown that complete power does not exist before the beginning, and the whole has been attributed to it. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage establishes the relationship between divine power (qudra) and actions, using the analogy of seeing a thing being thrown or taken out. Maturidi argues that just as we see the throwing and removal together with the thing itself without nullifying each other, so we should understand power as being seen by God together with the action at the moment of its existence. He then summarizes that an action has three temporal states: before it (non-existence), after it (annihilation), and at its moment (existence). God sees the action in all its states, but power is seen only with the action in its state of existence, not before or after. He then addresses an opponent's proof using the Qur'anic verse (2:282) about inability to dictate, interpreting it as referring to inability due to lack of knowledge or incapacity (istita'at al-'ajz). The proof is that complete power (qudra al-tamam) does not exist before the beginning (al-ibtida'), and the whole has been attributed to it, concluding again that there is no power except by God.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
الفعل (al-fi'l)
العدم (al-'adam)
الفناء (al-fana')
الوجود (al-wujud)
استطاعة العجز (istita'at al-'ajz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think power is a separate entity existing before the action.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that power is seen by God only with the action at the moment of its existence, not before or after.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, in which state does God see power (qudra) with respect to an action?
A) Before the action
B) At the moment of the action's existence
C) After the action
D) In all three states equally

Key Takeaway
Divine power is not a pre-existing cause but is seen by God only together with the action at its moment of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan for the future, do you assume that your ability to act already exists within you? How would your daily reliance on Allah change if you truly believed that your power is created only at the moment of action?
?
Think of a time you delayed doing something good, telling yourself you would do it later. Did you assume your future self would have the same ability? How does this lesson expose that assumption as a form of ignoring your total dependence on Allah's moment-by-moment creation of your power?
?
Is there an area in your life where you feel 'powerless' right now? What would it mean to stop waiting for that power to appear on its own, and instead turn to Allah, asking Him to grant you the ability at the moment you need to act?

The First Aspect: Uniqueness of Power
Maturidi presents the initial objection and the first aspect: uniqueness of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the opponent's own argument against dualists to refute the claim that human power resembles divine power.

Core Idea
The argument of uniqueness (الانفراد) applies equally to the opponent.

Maturidi first presents the opponent's objection that affirming power for humans is tashbih. He then offers a preliminary reply, but his deeper analysis reveals a self-contradiction in the opponent's position. The opponent had previously used the uniqueness of divine power to refute the dualists; now that same argument returns against him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: objection, reply, then Maturidi's analysis.
2
Identify the first aspect
Find the phrase 'أحدهما الانفراد بالقدرة' and understand its meaning: the uniqueness of power.
3
Recognize the self-contradiction
The opponent used the uniqueness argument against dualists; now he must face it regarding human power. The text says: 'وبه احتججت في نقض قول الثنوية، فيلزمك في هذا.'
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this argument work? Because if human power were truly independent, it would challenge God's unique power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر سؤال خصمه من وجه لا يحتمل خصمه أن يقول به، فقال: إذ أثبتم لأنفسكم القدرة فقد أشبهتم الله بها فقال: لا يجب ذا لما قدرت به، وهو يقدر لا بغيره، كما يقال في العلم. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: لو قدرت بالله لم يجز أن تزول بقدرتك قدرة الله، كما إذ علمت به لم يزل بعلمك علم الله به وبعد، فإن السؤال من وجهين. أحدهما الانفراد بالقدرة، وبه احتججت في نقض قول الثنوية، فيلزمك في هذا. والثاني أن ذلك يوجب الغنى عن الله في الفعل قبل وجوده، ولا يجوز أن يكون الله يُغنى أحداً عن نفسه. فإن قلت: يحتاج إليه في الإبقاء أحلت عندك، لأنها لا تحتمل. وإن قلت: يُحدث أخرى، فقد أغناه عنه في وقت، ولو جاز ذلك في الوقت مع قيام العبودة يجوز أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he mentioned his opponent's question in a manner that his opponent cannot say, and said: 'When you affirm power for yourselves, you have resembled Allah with it.' He said: 'It is not necessary, because you have power by which you act, while He (Allah) is powerful not by something other than Himself, as is said regarding knowledge.' Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'If you had power by Allah, it would not be permissible that your power removes Allah's power, just as when you know by Him, your knowledge does not remove Allah's knowledge of Him and after. Then the question has two aspects. One is the uniqueness of power, and by this you argued in refuting the statement of the Dualists, so it applies to you here. The second is that it entails independence from Allah in the act before its existence, and it is not permissible that Allah makes someone independent of Himself. If you say: He needs it for preservation, you would be stumped, because it does not persist. And if you say: He creates another (power), then He has made him independent of Him in that time, and if that is permissible in one time despite the establishment of servitude, it would be permissible forever. And there is no power except with Allah.'
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that affirming a power for humans is a form of tashbih (likening creation to the Creator). The opponent asserts that if humans have power, they resemble Allah. Maturidi first quotes a possible reply: divine power is not through another, whereas human power is. But then Maturidi gives a deeper answer: if human power were from Allah, it would not negate Allah's power, just as knowledge from Allah does not negate divine knowledge. He then analyzes the objection from two angles. First, the claim of uniqueness of power (الانفراد بالقدرة) – the opponent himself used this argument against the Dualists, so it now applies against his own position. Second, affirming an independent human power would imply independence from Allah in the act before its occurrence, which violates tawhid. Maturidi then considers two possible defenses: (1) that the power needs Allah for preservation – but powers are accidents (أعراض) and do not persist, so this fails; (2) that Allah creates a new power each time – but that would still grant independence in that moment, and if allowed once, it would be allowed always, destroying the concept of servitude (عبودية). The argument concludes with 'لا قوة إلا بالله' (there is no power except with Allah).

Key Terms
قدرة
انفراد
ثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human power is completely independent from God and thus resembles divine power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that human power is not independent; it is given by God and does not rival His unique power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how does the opponent's argument against the Dualists apply to the opponent himself?
A) The opponent used the uniqueness of power to refute dualists, now it applies to his own claim about human power.
B) The opponent denied human power, but dualists affirmed it.
C) The opponent claimed power is an accident.
D) The opponent used analogical reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The uniqueness of divine power (الانفراد) is a key argument that the opponent cannot evade without contradicting himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when do you feel most in control—as if your power is entirely your own? How would your actions change if you constantly reminded yourself that your power is never independent of God?
?
Consider a moment when you took full credit for an achievement. Did you actually believe your effort alone caused it? What would it look like to genuinely attribute that success to God's power while still acknowledging your own role?
?
The opponent in this passage used an argument against others but failed to apply it to himself. Are there areas in your faith or practice where you hold others to a standard but make exceptions for yourself? Will you commit today to recognizing one such blind spot?

The Second Aspect: Independence from Allah
Maturidi discusses the second aspect: independence from Allah, and refutes two defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming an independent human power would imply independence from Allah in the act, and how Maturidi refutes two possible counterarguments.

Core Idea
If human power were independent even for a moment, it would destroy servitude.

Maturidi now turns to the second aspect of the objection: that an independent human power would make humans independent of Allah in their actions before those actions occur. This directly contradicts tawhid. Maturidi then anticipates two possible defenses and shows their failure, concluding that no power exists except with Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the second aspect and the two defenses that Maturidi refutes.
2
Understand the second aspect
The claim is that affirming human power leads to independence from Allah in the act before its existence.
3
Analyze the first defense
The opponent says power needs Allah for preservation. Maturidi replies that power is an accident and does not persist.
4
Analyze the second defense
The opponent says Allah creates a new power each time. Maturidi replies that this grants temporary independence, and if allowed once, it would be allowed forever.
5
Conclude with the final declaration
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' (there is no power except with Allah), summarizing total dependence.

Then he mentioned his opponent's question in a manner that his opponent cannot say, and said: 'When you affirm power for yourselves, you have resembled Allah with it.' And there is no power except with Allah.'

The opponent asserts that if humans have power, they resemble Allah. Maturidi first quotes a possible reply: divine power is not through another, whereas human power is. إبقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah creates power in humans, humans can act independently without Allah's direct involvement.
Correction: Maturidi argues that any power, even if created, is not independent; it is always dependent on Allah for its existence and the act's occurrence. The idea of independence even for a moment is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's response to the claim that the power needs Allah for preservation?
A) Power is eternal, so it does not need preservation.
B) Power is an accident and does not persist, so it cannot be preserved.
C) Power is self-subsisting.
D) Allah does not preserve power.

Key Takeaway
True tawhid requires that human power is not independent even for a moment; all power is from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do a good deed, do you credit your own ability or thank Allah for the power to do it? How does this passage challenge your hidden pride?
?
Think of a moment you felt completely self-sufficient. Now realize that even that feeling was sustained by Allah. What would it take for you to live every moment with 'there is no power except with Allah' on your tongue?
?
If you truly believe that your power is not independent even for a second, how must your prayer and trust in Allah change today?

The Opponent's Objection on Power Resembling God and the Two Aspects
Maturidi refutes the opponent's objection regarding resemblance of human power to God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dialectical method in refuting the claim that human power resembles God's power, and the two aspects of the objection.

Core Idea
Human power is derived from God, so resemblance does not diminish God's unique power.

Maturidi presents an opponent's challenge: if humans have power, they resemble God. In this passage, he refutes the objection by distinguishing between derived and essential power, and exposes two problematic aspects of the claim. This lesson unpacks the dialectical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed dialectical style.
2
Identify the opponent's objection and Maturidi's initial counter
Spot the objection ('إذ أثبتم لأنفسكم القدرة فقد أشبهتم الله بها') and the initial response ('لا يجب ذا لما قدرت به'). Understand the analogy with knowledge.
3
Understand the two aspects of the objection
Maturidi says the question has two aspects: uniqueness in power (الانفراد بالقدرة) and independence from God (الغنى عن الله). See how each is argued.
The first aspect turns the opponent's own argument against dualists back on them.
4
Analyze the principle that power exists only for an act
Maturidi states the foundational principle: 'القدرة محال كونها لا للفعل' (power cannot exist except for an act). Understand how this refutes the idea of power preceding the act.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that reason forces abandonment of the doctrine of precedence. Consider how this argument defends divine uniqueness and the dependent nature of human power.
This lesson shows the sophistication of Maturidi's theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر سؤال خصمه من وجه لا يحتمل خصمه أن يقول به، فقال: إذ أثبتم لأنفسكم القدرة فقد أشبهتم الله بها فقال : لا يجب ذا لما قدرت به، وهو يقدر لا بغيره، كما يقال في العلم. { قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : } لو قدرت بالله لم يجز أن تزول بقدرتك قدرة الله ، كما إذ علمت به لم يزل بعلمك علم الله به وبعد، فإن السؤال من وجهين. أحدهما الانفراد بالقدرة، وبه احتججت في نقض قول الثنوية، فيلزمك في هذا . والثاني أن ذلك يوجب الغنى عن الله في الفعل قبل وجوده ، ولا يجوز أن يكون الله يُغنى أحداً عن نفسه. فإن قلت: يحتاج إليه في الإبقاء أحلت عندك ، لأنها لا تحتمل. وإن قلت: يُحدث أخرى، فقد أغناه عنه في وقت، ولو جاز ذلك في الوقت مع قيام العبودة يجوز أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل أن القدرة محال كونها لا للفعل، وكذلك العجز لا عن فعل. ثم قد يجوز أن يكون قادرًا في وقت للفعل، يعجز في الوقت الثاني، إذ معلوم وجود مثله؛ فيكون الله تعالى معطيّا القوة لشيء يستحيل كونه ، وفي ذلك فساد كون القوة للفعل. فألزم ما أوجبه العقل أنها لا تكون إلا للفعل إحالـة القول بالتقدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he mentioned the question of his opponent in a manner that his opponent could not possibly say, so he said: Since you affirm for yourselves power, you have resembled God by it. He said: This is not necessary because of what you are empowered by, and He empowers not by other than Himself, just as is said regarding knowledge. {Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said:} If you were empowered by God, it would not be possible for God's power to be removed by your power, just as when you know by Him, God's knowledge of it is not removed by your knowledge, and afterwards, the question is from two aspects. One is uniqueness in power, and by it you argued in refuting the dualists, so it applies to you here. The second is that it requires independence from God in the act before its existence, and it is not permissible for God to make anyone independent of Himself. If you say: He needs Him for preservation, you are inconsistent according to you, because it does not persist. And if you say: He creates another, then He has made him independent of Him at a time, and if that is permissible at a time while servitude remains, it is permissible forever. And there is no power except through God. The principle is that it is impossible for power to exist not for an act, and likewise inability not from an act. Then it is possible for one to be powerful at a time for an act and be incapable at the second time, since the existence of its like is known; so God, exalted, would be granting power to something impossible for it to exist, and in that is the corruption of power being for the act. So what reason necessitated—that it cannot be except for the act—compels the abandonment of the doctrine of precedence. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. Maturidi first recounts an opponent's objection: if you (Muslims) affirm that humans have power (qudra), you make them resemble God, who is the ultimate powerful. The response is that this does not follow because human power is derived from God ('what you are empowered by' refers to the power granted by God) while God's power is unique. Maturidi then elaborates that the opponent's question has two aspects: (1) uniqueness in power—the same argument used against dualists can be turned against the objector; (2) the implication of independence from God in the act before existence—which is impossible because every creature needs God. He then examines possible replies from the opponent: if one says the creature needs God for preservation of power, that is inconsistent because power is an accident and does not persist; if the adversary says God creates a new power each time, then the creature is independent at that moment, which if allowed once could be allowed forever. Maturidi then states the foundational principle: power cannot exist except for an act, and inability cannot exist except regarding an act. He concludes that reason demands that power is only for the act, thereby refuting the theory of 'precedence' (taqaddum, likely the idea that power precedes the act).

Key Terms
القدرة
العلم
الانفراد
الغنى
الأصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'power' when applied to humans implies a similarity in essence and reality to God's power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that human power is derived from God and does not diminish or resemble God's power in its essential attributes, such as uniqueness and independence.

Checkpoint
What are the two aspects of the opponent's question that Maturidi identifies?
A) Uniqueness in power and independence from God.
B) Resemblance to God and denial of prophecy.
C) Creation of power and its persistence.
D) Power before the act and after the act.

Key Takeaway
Human power does not resemble God's power because it is derived from Him and is only for an act, thus preserving divine uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something you worked hard for, do you silently credit your own effort and ability, or do you truly feel that God's power was the sole enabler? What would change in your heart if you internalized that your power is merely a created, dependent gift? Today, before you start any task, consciously remind yourself: 'لا قوة إلا بالله'—and see if it shifts your sense of self-reliance.
?
The lesson shows that even a momentary feeling of independence from God is logically unsustainable and contradicts servitude. Where in your life have you nurtured a pocket of independence—perhaps in your skills, relationships, or daily routines—that you have not surrendered to God? What is one concrete step you can take today to acknowledge your utter need for Him in that area?
?
If your power exists only at the moment of the act and is directly given by God, then your very ability to perform a good deed is a fresh creation from Him. How should this reshape your gratitude and humility? Will you take time after your next prayer to thank God not just for the act, but for the very capacity to do it?

God's Power and Knowledge: Answering Objections
Maturidi presents and answers two objections about divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical distinction between God's power to create and His power over His own knowledge, and how Maturidi refutes Husayn's argument about God's knowledge of hypotheticals.

Core Idea
God's power does not extend to changing His own knowledge — it is eternal and fixed.

Maturidi continues his defense of divine attributes by addressing two further objections. The first concerns a logical implication: if God has power to create the world, does that mean He could also nullify His own knowledge? The second objection, attributed to Husayn al-Najjar, challenges the relationship between God's knowledge and free human acts. This lesson unpacks both arguments and their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the two objections introduced by 'ثم عارض'.
The first objection is about God's power, the second is from Husayn.
2
Identify each objection and its refutation
Find where Maturidi presents the objection and where he implies the answer. The first objection is refuted by analogy with the first case; the second by affirming that God's knowledge covers all possibilities.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Why can't God's power nullify His knowledge? Because divine attributes are distinct and eternal. Understanding this strengthens the broader kalam framework.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض بقدرة الله على إنشاء العالم ليُحال ، من غير أن يجوز الوصف بالقدرة على إبطال علمه، فمثله الأول. ثم عارض حسينًا بالإطلاق، إنه أطلق بينه وبين الإيمان . أتقول في أنه أطلق إبطال علم الله ؟ ثم قال: الله عالم أن لو كان كيف يكون، فلو كان لم يكن يخرج من علم الله .
Then he objected with God's power to create the world, that it would lead to [something] impossible, without it being permissible to describe [God] with power to nullify His knowledge — so it is like the first. Then he objected regarding Husayn's general statement: he made a general statement between him and belief. Do you say that he made a general statement of nullifying God's knowledge? Then he said: God knows how it would be if it were, so if it were, it would not leave God's knowledge.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage contains two further objections. The first objection is about God's own power: if God has power to create the world, does that imply He also has power to nullify His own knowledge? The answer is that it is similar to the first case — God's power to create does not entail power over His knowledge. The second objection is attributed to Husayn (likely Husayn al-Najjar). He made a general statement linking Pharaoh and belief, arguing that if God knows how something would be if it occurred, then it would still be within His knowledge even if it never occurs. The answer is implicit: God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging; what He knows will not occur cannot be changed.

Key Terms
قدرة الله
إنشاء العالم
علم الله
حسين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's power to create implies He could also change His knowledge.
Correction: God's power does not extend to His own attributes; His knowledge is eternal and unchanging, as shown by analogy with the first objection.

Checkpoint
What does the objection about God's power to create the world imply?
A) That God can nullify His knowledge.
B) That God's power is limited.
C) That God's knowledge is not eternal.
D) That God cannot create.

Key Takeaway
God's power and knowledge are distinct attributes; power to create does not imply power over knowledge, and God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities without change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly wish that God's knowledge of your past sins could be erased or changed, as if He didn't know? How does this passage's insistence that His knowledge is fixed and unchanging confront that desire? What does it mean for you to trust that His knowledge is perfect and you cannot escape it, nor do you need to?
?
The passage says God knows how things would be if they were—even things that never happen. When you look at your own life, what 'if only' scenarios do you hold onto? Are you willing to release them, knowing that God's knowledge of what could have been is already complete and good? What do you need to surrender today?

Refutation of the Foreknowledge Objection
Maturidi refutes an objection about God's foreknowledge of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that God's foreknowledge of belief compels belief, by exposing the dilemma and affirming the immutability of divine knowledge.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge does not compel; the objection collapses on a false dilemma.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to a common objection against predestination: if God already knows who will believe, then belief is compelled. Maturidi dismantles this objection by showing it leads to absurdity, either making God ignorant or creating a contradiction. He also emphasizes that God's knowledge is unchangeable, so the objection is misplaced. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully.
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the gist of the argument before diving into commentary.
Pay attention to the structure: dilemma, responses, conclusion.
2
Identify the dilemma presented.
Find where Maturidi asks: does God know that the person will not believe or not? This is the core fork of the argument.
The two horns are 'Yes' or 'No'.
3
Follow the refutation of each horn.
See how each answer leads to a problem: saying 'No' implies ignorance, saying 'Yes' creates a contradiction because God's knowledge says he won't believe, yet belief is demanded.
The phrase 'سفّهه' (makes him foolish) is key for the first horn.
4
Understand the immutability point.
Notice the final clause: 'if he believed, he would not exit from His knowledge'. This shows that God's knowledge is unchanging and encompasses all possibilities, so the objection fails.
The phrase 'ولقد ذكرت' refers back to an earlier argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: يعلم أنه آمن كيف يكون، فهذا معنى لا منفعة فيه، لأنه مع علمه بذلك يعلم أنه لا يؤمن أو لا؟ فإن قال: لا، سفّهه، وإن قال: نعم، قيل: في ذلك وقعت المطالبة، وقد ذكرت أنه لو آمن لم يخرج من علمه، فكيف لم يخرج، وعلمه أنه لا يكون، وقد كان؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And his statement: He knows that he believed, how is that? This is a meaning with no benefit, because with His knowledge of that, does He know that he will not believe or not? If he says: No, he makes him foolish; if he says: Yes, it is said: in that fell the demand, and I mentioned that if he believed, he would not exit from His knowledge, so how did he not exit, while His knowledge that it will not be had already existed? There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about God's foreknowledge. The objection is that if God knows that a person will believe, then that belief is inevitable. The response is that this kind of knowledge is meaningless because God knows whether that person will believe or not. If the objector says God does not know whether he will believe, then that makes God ignorant; if he says God knows, then the problem is that God knows that he will not believe, so the demand that he should believe is contradictory. The last part mentions that if he were to believe, he would not leave God's knowledge, so how could he leave it? So the argument is that God's knowledge is unchangeable, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
علم
إيمان
علمه
سفّه
مطالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge of a person's belief means that belief is compelled and the person has no free will.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's knowledge does not compel; rather, the objection is based on a false dilemma. God's knowledge is immutable and encompasses what will happen, but that knowledge does not cause the action.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma Maturidi presents to refute the foreknowledge objection?
A) God does not know whether the person will not believe, which would imply ignorance.
B) God knows that the person will believe, so belief is compelled.
C) The objector is foolish for raising the objection.
D) Belief is beneficial regardless of God's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the objection from foreknowledge is incoherent: God's knowledge is perfect and immutable, so it does not create compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself making excuses for sin by saying 'Allah already knows what I will do,' how does Maturidi's argument expose the fallacy in that thinking? What will you tell yourself next time that thought arises?
?
If you truly internalized that God's foreknowledge does not compel your actions, which area of your life would you immediately stop treating as 'fate' and start taking responsibility for?
?
The objectors in this passage used God's knowledge to justify their refusal to believe. Are there any truths you avoid acting on because you feel 'it won't make a difference'? How can Maturidi's refutation push you to act now?

Refuting the Claim that Powerlessness Removes Blame
Maturidi rejects the claim that lack of power removes blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that lack of power does not remove blame but rather increases it, and why the objection fails.

Core Idea
Lack of power does not remove blame; it amplifies it.

Maturidi responds to an objection that if a person had no ability, they wouldn't be blameworthy. In this passage, he turns the argument on its head, showing that the person is even more blameworthy for misusing the power they possess. He also clarifies why the distinction between power and faith does not save the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and find the statement that is being refuted: 'If he had no power over it, he would not be blameworthy.' Notice how Maturidi frames it as similar to a previous argument.
The objection is about power (qudra) and blame (lawm).
2
Understand Maturidi's core response
Maturidi argues that the person is actually more blameworthy because he wasted the power he had. Focus on the phrase: 'بل عليه أعظم اللائمة، لما هو ضيّع القدرة'.
This reverses the objector's logic.
3
Examine the secondary refutations
Maturidi addresses two further points: the objector's claim that the conclusion doesn't follow because power is not faith, and the reliance on command (amr). Note how he turns each argument against the objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله: «لولم يقدر عليه لم يكن ملوما» فهو مثل القول سواء، ودليله أنه قوله. بل عليه أعظم اللائمة، لما هو ضيّع القدرة حيث أعرض عن الذي به يأتيه. وقوله «لا يلزمنا لأن القدرة غير الإيمان يُدفع أيضاً، فما فيه ما يمنع هذا اللزوم؛ بل إنما لزم ذلك لأن القدرة غير الإيمان. ثم اعتباره بالأمر فاسد لأنه استعباد به يظهر ذله وعبوديته، والقوة هي الغنى والعلو والرفعة؛ فهو الوجه الذي به يبطل ربوبية غير الله، وليس في الأمر ذلك. على أنه لو لم يكن أمر ولا نهى كان القول بـيؤمن ويكفر ويقدر ولا يقدر» لا معنى له. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
As for his statement: 'If he had no power over it, he would not be blameworthy', it is like the statement (previously considered), and his proof is his statement. Rather, upon him is greater blame, because he wasted the power when he turned away from that by which it comes to him. And his statement: 'It does not follow us because power is not faith' is also rebutted, for there is nothing in it to prevent this following; rather, it follows precisely because power is not faith. Then his consideration of command is corrupt because it is an enslavement by which his lowliness and servitude appear, while power is self-sufficiency, highness, and elevation. So it is the aspect by which the lordship of other than Allah is invalidated, but it is not in command. Moreover, if there were no command or prohibition, the statement 'he believes and disbelieves and is able and not able' would have no meaning. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that if a person had no power (ability) to do an act, he would not be blameworthy. The author argues that, on the contrary, the person is more blameworthy for wasting his power and turning away from what is required. The objector claims that the conclusion does not follow because power is different from faith, but the author replies that it is precisely because power is different from faith that the blame follows. Then the author critiques the reliance on command (أمر) as a basis for obligation, arguing that command implies subjugation, whereas power implies glory and lordship. Finally, he notes that without command and prohibition, the categories of belief/disbelief and ability would be meaningless.

Key Terms

ملوما
لائمة
إيمان
أمر
استعباد
قوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a person lacks the power to do an act, they cannot be blamed for not doing it.
Correction: Maturidi argues that lack of power does not remove blame; on the contrary, blame is greater when one wastes the power they have. The blameworthy act is turning away from the source of power (Allah).

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does lack of power not remove blame?
A) Because blame is based on intention, not ability.
B) Because the person is more blameworthy for wasting the power they had.
C) Because power is irrelevant to moral responsibility.
D) Because command alone determines blame.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that blameworthiness increases when one misuses the power granted by Allah, and that the objection fails by conflating power with faith and misinterpreting command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How have you wasted the power Allah gave you—your time, health, intellect—by turning away from the One who granted it? Will you repent today for every ability misused?
?
When you say 'I can't' to an act of obedience, are you really unable, or have you simply chosen to turn away? What would it cost you to acknowledge that all power is from Allah and act accordingly?
?
If command and prohibition were removed, would your life still have meaning? Do your actions reflect that you are a servant, or do you live as if you are autonomous? What change must you make to live in constant reliance on 'there is no power except with Allah'?
