Divine Power and Mutual Exclusivity
Imam Maturidi answers an objection about divine and human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power is not subject to mutual exclusivity unlike human power.

Core Idea
God's power is unrestricted; human power is limited by opposite actions.

This passage addresses a key objection about divine and human agency. The objector asks why humans cannot exist without acting, while God can. Imam Maturidi explains that mutual exclusivity applies to humans but not to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the passage from the start until 'وغير ذلك' to understand the objection and Maturidi's first response.
Focus on the contrast between divine and human power regarding mutual exclusivity.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that God is described as having power over what has no opposite, while humans do not. This is the core difference.
3
Analyze the sub-objection and response
The text includes a further exchange about mutual exclusivity over time. Try to follow the logic of the argument.
The final sentence criticizes those who equate God's action with creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأجاب عن الأول أن الله كذلك بما لا تتضاد عليه الأفعال ويقدر على ما لا ضد له، والعبد لا يقدر على ما لا ضد له، لذلك لم يجز أن يوجد أوقاتًا غير فاعل. فيقال: وما فيها ذكرت ما قوبلت به بل قيل لك : ما منع أن يكون من تتضاد عليه لا يجوز وجوده ولا فعل شيء [موجود] أو ضده وقتا واحدا؟ ومن لا تتضاد عليه يجوز. ثم يقال للتضاد: لا يجيز وقت القدرة، أله [أن] يوجب [ذلك] في الوقت الثاني ؟ فأي الأمرين أجاب فهو في الحالين واحد وما قال على الله فهو فاسد، لما ليس عنده فعل الله غير خلقه، وهو متضاد كالموت والحياة وغير ذلك.
ثم أجاب بأول أحوال الجسم أنه يخلو عن الحركة والسكون، لم لم يجب به خلاؤه عنهما أوقاتا ؟ قال ابو منصور رحمه الله : فنقول وبالله التوفيق: الحركة والسكون هما اسما البقاء، فمحال وجودهما في أول أحوال الجسم لإحالة البقاء، إذ السكون هو القرار حيث الوجود، والحركة الانتقال عنه، والقدرة ليست إلا للفعل. ولو جاز وجودها ولا فعل وقتا واحدا لجاز أوقاتا، إذ هي له. والجسم ليس للحركة ولا للسكون، وهما معنيان لا يقتضيان الحال. ألا يُرى أن أوقات البقاء لا تخلو عنهما، ثم القدرة لا تبقى فيجب أن لا تخلو منه عند الوجود ولا قوة إلا بالله.
وبعد فإن مسألتنا في الفعل، ونجيز من الجسم وقت وجوده إذا لم يكن الفعل الذي هو اسم للبقاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He answered the first [objection] that God is such that actions are not mutually exclusive upon Him and He has power over what has no opposite, while the servant does not have power over what has no opposite. Therefore it is not permissible that he exists at times as non-actor. It is said: What did you mention? It was said to you: What prevents that one upon whom actions are mutually exclusive cannot exist and [cannot] do a thing or its opposite at one time? And one upon whom they are not mutually exclusive is permissible. Then it is said regarding mutual exclusivity: It does not permit at the time of power; is it necessary that it imposes that in the second time? Whichever of the two matters he answers, he is in both states one, and what he says about God is corrupt, because according to him the action of God is nothing other than His creation, and it is mutually exclusive like death and life and other than that. Then he answered regarding the first states of the body that it is empty of motion and rest: why is its emptiness of them at times not necessary? Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: Motion and rest are the two names of continuity, so their existence in the first states of the body is impossible because of the impossibility of continuity. For rest is remaining where existence is, and motion is transition from it. Power is only for action. If its existence were permissible without action at one time, it would be permissible at times, because it is for it. The body is not for motion nor for rest, and they are two meanings that do not necessitate the state. Do you not see that times of continuity are not empty of them? Then power does not remain, so it must not be empty of it at the moment of existence, and there is no power except by God. After that, our question is about action, and we permit for the body at the time of its existence, if the action which is the name of continuity is not [present]. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection about divine and human agency. It first establishes that God's actions are not mutually exclusive (تضاد) in the way human actions are, because God has power over what has no opposite, whereas humans lack that. The objector asks why a human cannot exist without being an actor (i.e., without performing some action), while God can. The answer is that for beings subject to mutual exclusivity (like humans), it is impossible to exist without either an action or its opposite at a given time, but for God it is permissible. Then the discussion turns to the first state of a body (before it acquires motion or rest). The objection asks why the body cannot be empty of both motion and rest at all times. Abu Mansur (Imam Maturidi) explains that motion and rest are names for continuity (بقاء) of existence. Rest is remaining in place, motion is leaving it. Power is only for action, so if it were possible to have power without action at one instant, it would be possible at all instants, because power is for action. However, the body is not inherently for motion or rest; they are two meanings that do not necessitate a state. Observation shows that times of continuity are never empty of motion or rest. Since power does not persist (i.e., it is created anew each moment), it must not be without action at the moment of existence. Finally, the passage clarifies that the topic is action, and permits the body to be without action at the first moment of its existence, if the action that is the name of continuity is absent. The phrase 'there is no power except by God' emphasizes divine reliance.

Key Terms
تضاد
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is limited by opposites like human power.
Correction: God has power over what has no opposite, humans do not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can a human not exist at a time without being an actor?
A) Because human power is limited.
B) Because human actions are mutually exclusive.
C) Because humans cannot have power without action.
D) Because God creates all actions.

Key Takeaway
God's power is free from mutual exclusivity, unlike human power which is constrained by opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly believe that God's power is limited like yours—that He cannot hold all things together or bring about good from opposite situations? How does recognizing that God is free from mutual exclusivity change the way you pray in moments of confusion? This week, whenever you face a contradiction in life, verbally affirm that God's power is not bound by opposites.
?
When you sin, do you act as if God's mercy and justice are mutually exclusive—as if He can only be one or the other? How does the truth that God's attributes are not mutually exclusive challenge your fear of repentance? Commit to memorizing the phrase 'actions are not mutually exclusive upon Him' and repeat it when you doubt His forgiveness.
?
Your own power is limited by mutual exclusivity: you cannot be in two places at once or do opposite actions simultaneously. How does accepting this limitation humble you and drive you to rely on God's unlimited power? What is one area of your life where you are trying to be 'self-sufficient' like God, and how can you surrender it to Him today?

Motion, Rest, and the Nature of Action
Imam Maturidi explains motion/rest as continuity and refutes emptiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's argument that motion and rest are names of continuity and why a body cannot be without either at the moment of existence.

Core Idea
Motion and rest define a body's continuity; power requires action.

Imam Maturidi explains the nature of motion and rest as attributes of continuity. He argues that it is impossible for a body to be without motion or rest, and power is always for action.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about the first state of the body
Start from 'ثم أجاب' and read Maturidi's response. The objection is: why can the body not be empty of motion and rest at all times?
The answer involves the concept of continuity (بقاء).
2
Understand the definition of motion and rest
Maturidi defines motion and rest as names of continuity. Rest is remaining in place, motion is transition. This is crucial.
Continuity means persisting in existence.
3
Grasp the implication for power and action
Power requires action. If power could exist without action, it would be permissible always. But observation shows continuity requires motion or rest.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' emphasizes divine reliance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأجاب عن الأول أن الله كذلك بما لا تتضاد عليه الأفعال ويقدر على ما لا ضد له، والعبد لا يقدر على ما لا ضد له، لذلك لم يجز أن يوجد أوقاتًا غير فاعل. فيقال: وما فيها ذكرت ما قوبلت به بل قيل لك : ما منع أن يكون من تتضاد عليه لا يجوز وجوده ولا فعل شيء [موجود] أو ضده وقتا واحدا؟ ومن لا تتضاد عليه يجوز. ثم يقال للتضاد: لا يجيز وقت القدرة، أله [أن] يوجب [ذلك] في الوقت الثاني ؟ فأي الأمرين أجاب فهو في الحالين واحد وما قال على الله فهو فاسد، لما ليس عنده فعل الله غير خلقه، وهو متضاد كالموت والحياة وغير ذلك.
ثم أجاب بأول أحوال الجسم أنه يخلو عن الحركة والسكون، لم لم يجب به خلاؤه عنهما أوقاتا ؟ قال ابو منصور رحمه الله : فنقول وبالله التوفيق: الحركة والسكون هما اسما البقاء، فمحال وجودهما في أول أحوال الجسم لإحالة البقاء، إذ السكون هو القرار حيث الوجود، والحركة الانتقال عنه، والقدرة ليست إلا للفعل. ولو جاز وجودها ولا فعل وقتا واحدا لجاز أوقاتا، إذ هي له. والجسم ليس للحركة ولا للسكون، وهما معنيان لا يقتضيان الحال. ألا يُرى أن أوقات البقاء لا تخلو عنهما، ثم القدرة لا تبقى فيجب أن لا تخلو منه عند الوجود ولا قوة إلا بالله.
وبعد فإن مسألتنا في الفعل، ونجيز من الجسم وقت وجوده إذا لم يكن الفعل الذي هو اسم للبقاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He answered the first [objection] that God is such that actions are not mutually exclusive upon Him and He has power over what has no opposite, while the servant does not have power over what has no opposite. Therefore it is not permissible that he exists at times as non-actor. It is said: What did you mention? It was said to you: What prevents that one upon whom actions are mutually exclusive cannot exist and [cannot] do a thing or its opposite at one time? And one upon whom they are not mutually exclusive is permissible. Then it is said regarding mutual exclusivity: It does not permit at the time of power; is it necessary that it imposes that in the second time? Whichever of the two matters he answers, he is in both states one, and what he says about God is corrupt, because according to him the action of God is nothing other than His creation, and it is mutually exclusive like death and life and other than that. Then he answered regarding the first states of the body that it is empty of motion and rest: why is its emptiness of them at times not necessary? Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: Motion and rest are the two names of continuity, so their existence in the first states of the body is impossible because of the impossibility of continuity. For rest is remaining where existence is, and motion is transition from it. Power is only for action. If its existence were permissible without action at one time, it would be permissible at times, because it is for it. The body is not for motion nor for rest, and they are two meanings that do not necessitate the state. Do you not see that times of continuity are not empty of them? Then power does not remain, so it must not be empty of it at the moment of existence, and there is no power except by God. After that, our question is about action, and we permit for the body at the time of its existence, if the action which is the name of continuity is not [present]. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection about divine and human agency. It first establishes that God's actions are not mutually exclusive (تضاد) in the way human actions are, because God has power over what has no opposite, whereas humans lack that. The objector asks why a human cannot exist without being an actor (i.e., without performing some action), while God can. The answer is that for beings subject to mutual exclusivity (like humans), it is impossible to exist without either an action or its opposite at a given time, but for God it is permissible. Then the discussion turns to the first state of a body (before it acquires motion or rest). The objection asks why the body cannot be empty of both motion and rest at all times. Abu Mansur (Imam Maturidi) explains that motion and rest are names for continuity (بقاء) of existence. Rest is remaining in place, motion is leaving it. Power is only for action, so if it were possible to have power without action at one instant, it would be possible at all instants, because power is for action. However, the body is not inherently for motion or rest; they are two meanings that do not necessitate a state. Observation shows that times of continuity are never empty of motion or rest. Since power does not persist (i.e., it is created anew each moment), it must not be without action at the moment of existence. Finally, the passage clarifies that the topic is action, and permits the body to be without action at the first moment of its existence, if the action that is the name of continuity is absent. The phrase 'there is no power except by God' emphasizes divine reliance.

Correction: Motion and rest are names of continuity; a body cannot be without them at any moment of existence.

Refuting the Objection from Self-Sufficiency and Power
Maturidi refutes the analogy between power and wealth regarding self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the temporary nature of human power proves dependence on God rather than independence.

Core Idea
Power that does not last reveals need, not self-sufficiency.

In this passage, Maturidi tackles an objection that tries to use the concept of self-sufficiency (ghinā) to argue against divine control over human actions. The opponent claims that if human power were from God, then the human would be independent. Maturidi responds by showing that the very nature of power as a non-permanent accident demonstrates the opposite: constant need for God. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the gist of the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
Find the part where the opponent tries to equate power with self-sufficiency and uses the sword/dirham analogy.
The opponent says 'God forbid' because he thinks power implies independence.
3
Understand Maturidi's first point
Maturidi says power does not admit permanence; it is temporary, thus necessitating need.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Analyze the second point
Existence of power does not perpetuate action; only divine preservation does.
This refutes the idea that power alone suffices.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection backfires: the opponent's own criteria lead to the very conclusion he sought to avoid.
The phrase 'Therefore, what you sought refuge from is incumbent upon you' sums it up.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالغنى، فقال: معاذ الله لأنه المغنى. وعارض بالسيف والدرهم. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: } وقد حاد عن ذلك، إذ القوة لا تحتمل البقاء، وبها أوجب الحاجة، فإذا استحال ذلك لزم ما عورض به عن الغنى ولا قوة إلا بالله. وما قابله به فالوجود لا يديم الفعل، بل يديمه البقاء، وله الحاجة إلى الإبقاء، وليس ذلك في القوة؛ فلذلك لزمك الذي تعوذت منه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected with self-sufficiency, saying: God forbid, for He is the Enricher. And he objected with the sword and the dirham. The Sheikh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: He deviated from that, for power does not admit permanence, and by it He necessitated need. Since that is impossible, what he objected with regarding self-sufficiency follows. There is no power except through Allah. And what he countered with—existence does not perpetuate the action; rather, permanence perpetuates it, and it (the action) has need of preservation, and that is not in the power. Therefore, what you sought refuge from is incumbent upon you. There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
The opponent tries to argue from the concept of self-sufficiency (ghinā): if power were from God, God would be the only one self-sufficient, but the servant's power would imply independence. He also uses the sword/dirham example. Maturidi replies that the opponent has deviated from the point: power (qudra) is an accident that does not remain; it is created at each moment. Since it does not last, it actually demonstrates the servant's need for God, not independence. Moreover, existence (wujūd) of the power at the first moment does not perpetuate the action; only the continued existence (baqā') of the action does, and that continuance itself requires God's preservation. Thus, the very thing the opponent sought refuge from—attributing independence to the creature—actually applies to his own analogy.

بقاء

Correction: Power is an accident that does not persist; therefore it shows constant need for God's creation and preservation.

B) Power is an accident that does not last, showing need.
C) The opponent admits God is the Enricher.
D) Power is self-subsisting.

Key Takeaway
Human power is inherently temporary, hence a mark of need, not independence; only God is truly Self-Sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a moment when you felt truly capable—perhaps you succeeded at something difficult, or someone praised your skill. In that moment, did you feel self-sufficient? This lesson says that very feeling of power actually proves your need. How will you now respond the next time you catch yourself feeling proud of your own ability? Be specific: what will you say to yourself or to Allah?
?
The opponent in this passage tried to argue that attributing power to God would make the creature independent. Maturidi flipped it: the temporary nature of power proves dependence. Have you ever resisted fully relying on God because you thought it would make you passive or irresponsible? After reading this, can you see that true reliance is not passivity but the only realistic posture? What is one area of your life where you secretly still trust your own power more than Allah’s preservation?

Refutation of the Independence Objection
Maturidi refutes the claim that specific capacity implies independence from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the claim that the specificity of capacity implies independence from God, and why power (qudra) entails need rather than self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Power creates need; independence cannot be claimed for contingent beings.

Imam Maturidi here addresses a subtle objection: if God's power is specific to each action, could that imply a kind of independence? The opponent tries to avoid the implication by taking refuge in God's attribute of being the Enricher. Maturidi exposes the logical flaw in that reasoning, showing that power (qudra) itself is a sign of need, not autonomy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the opponent's retreat: 'God forbid' and the counter-examples with sword and dirham.
2
Identify the two sides of the argument
Distinguish the opponent's objection about independence and Maturidi's response. Mark where each begins.
The opponent speaks first with 'ثم عارض نفسه بالغنى' and then 'معاذ الله'.
3
Understand key terms
Grasp the meaning of qudra (power), baqa (continuance), haja (need), and ibqa (preservation). These are central to the refutation.
Power (qudra) does not include continuance; it is the basis of need.
4
Grasp the logical move
Maturidi argues that power necessitates need, and independence would require self-sustaining continuance, which no created power possesses. Therefore the opponent's refuge fails.
The final 'no power except by Allah' reinforces that all power is from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالغنى، فقال: معاذ الله لأنه المغنى. وعارض بالسيف والدرهم. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وقد حاد عن ذلك، إذ القوة لا تحتمل البقاء، وبها أوجب الحاجة، فإذا استحال ذلك لزم ما عورض به عن الغنى ولا قوة إلا بالله. وما قابله به فالوجود لا يديم الفعل، بل يديمه البقاء، وله الحاجة إلى الإبقاء، وليس ذلك في القوة؛ فلذلك لزمك الذي تعوذت منه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to himself by independence, and said: God forbid because He is the Enricher. And he objected with the sword and dirham. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: He has deviated from that, because power does not admit continuance, and by it He necessitated need, so if that is impossible, what was objected by independence follows, and no power except by Allah. And what he countered with is that existence does not perpetuate the action, rather continuance perpetuates it, and He has need for preservation, and that is not in power; therefore what you sought refuge from becomes necessary for you. And no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that if capacity is specific to one action, it might imply independence (ghina) from God. The opponent says 'God forbid' because God is the Enricher (al-Mughni). The Shaykh (Maturidi) responds that the opponent has deviated from the issue. Power (qudra) does not admit continuance (baqa'); rather, by power God creates need. Therefore, if independence were possible, it would entail the very thing the opponent took refuge from. Furthermore, existence (wujud) does not sustain the action; continuance sustains it, and need for preservation remains. This is not in power itself, so the opponent's refuge is invalid.

Correction: Power (qudra) actually entails need, not independence; continuance and preservation are beyond created power.

Without God's preservation, it would vanish. Will you let this truth humble you into gratitude, or will you continue to act as if your accomplishments stand on your own power? Make a concrete decision right now to acknowledge His preservation in one specific area.

Maturidi's Refutation of Conditional Outcomes in the Tablet
Maturidi refutes the claim that God's foreknowledge implies contradictory possibilities in the tablet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's knowledge is certain and does not involve conditional possibilities, and the distinction between the Preserved Tablet and the 'wasted tablet'.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is certain; the Preserved Tablet records only definite outcomes.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to an opponent who claims that God's foreknowledge of future events implies that the Preserved Tablet contains conditional statements. Maturidi argues that such conditional writing stems from ignorance, not divine knowledge, and he distinguishes between the true Preserved Tablet and a false 'wasted tablet' that reflects human uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the forceful rhetorical style Maturidi uses to refute the opponent.
The passage contains a central question-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and Maturidi's response
The opponent seems to think God writes conditional outcomes in the Tablet. Maturidi's response shows why that is inconsistent with God's certain knowledge.
3
Analyze the question about God's knowledge of killing
Trace Maturidi's question: Does God know if a person will be killed? If yes, then how can the killing end his life if God knew it? This refutes the idea of conditional outcomes.
4
Distinguish between the two tablets
Find where Maturidi contrasts the Preserved Tablet with the 'wasted tablet'. Understand why conditional writing belongs to the latter.
The wasted tablet represents human speculation, not God's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من تأمل ما قال وما قوبل به أيقن أنه حائد حد عن الجواب . لكنا نذكر غفلته فيما حايد به ليعلموا عذره في جميع ما فارق به خصومه؛ إذ هذا مبلغ علمه في الله سبحانه. ونقول له : الله علم أنه يُقتل أو لا؟ فإن قال: يعلم، قيل: وقتله يزيل حياته ويذهب عمره أو لا؟ فإن قال لا كذبه الوجود وإن قال: نعم، قيل: كيف جعل انقضاء عمره وخروج روحه من جسده بغيره، لو علم ذلك؟ وكيف كتب في اللوح أنه إن فعل كذا يكون كذا، وإن لم يفعل كذا يكون كذا؟ وهذا أمر من لا يعلم ما يكون. فأما من يعلم ما يكون فهو يكتب : يكون كذا ، ولولا أنه يكون [كان] كذا؛ وكذا يكفر فلان مقت الله ولولا أنه يكفر كان يؤمن ويستوجب محبة الله. فأما القول بـ يكون ذا أو ذا من غير القطع بما يكون إنما هو فعل الجهال بالعواقب. ثم أنّى يكون ذا خبرًا عن علم يثبته قبل كونه ؟ وكل الناس يعلمون هذا القدر : إن فلاناً إما يُقتل أو يموت، يؤمن أو يكفر، يتحرك في وقت كذا أو يسكن . فهذا القدر من اللوح هو لوح كل سفينة وليس هو اللوح المحفوظ، ولكنه اللوح المضيع. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Whoever considers what he said and what was said in response will be certain that he deviated far from the answer. But we will mention his oversight in what he deviated by, so that they may know his excuse in all that he differed from his opponents; for this is the extent of his knowledge concerning God, exalted. And we say to him: Does God know whether he will be killed or not? If he says: He knows, it is said: Does his killing remove his life and take away his lifespan or not? If he says no, existence belies him, and if he says yes, it is said: How did He make the end of his lifespan and the exit of his soul from his body by another, if He knew that? And how did He write in the Tablet that if he does such-and-such, such-and-such will be, and if he does not do such-and-such, such-and-such will be? This is the action of one who does not know what will be. As for one who knows what will be, he writes: such-and-such will be, and were it not that it will be, [it would be] such-and-such; likewise, so-and-so disbelieves deserving God's wrath, and were it not that he disbelieves, he would believe and deserve God's love. As for the saying 'it will be this or that' without certainty of what will be, that is only the action of the ignorant of consequences. Then how can this be information about knowledge that He establishes before its occurrence? And all people know this much: that so-and-so will either be killed or die, believe or disbelieve, move at such a time or be still. So this much of the Tablet is the tablet of every ship, and it is not the Preserved Tablet, but rather the wasted tablet. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's view about God's foreknowledge and the content of the Preserved Tablet. Maturidi begins by stating that anyone who reflects on the opponent's statement and the response will see that he has deviated far from the correct answer. He then points out the opponent's oversight as an excuse for his disagreements. The core argument proceeds as a question: Does God know whether a person will be killed or not? If the opponent concedes that God knows, Maturidi asks: Does killing remove his life? If the opponent denies that, reality contradicts him; if he affirms it, then how can God—who knows the outcome—cause the end of life by another's act? This leads to the issue of the Tablet: if God writes conditional statements like 'if he does this, then this will be,' that implies ignorance of the future. But God knows definitively what will be, so He writes only one outcome. Maturidi distinguishes between the true Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz) and the 'wasted tablet' (al-lawh al-mudi') which contains mere possibilities, like the knowledge of ordinary people who only know that a person might be killed or die. This 'wasted tablet' is not real divine knowledge. The passage ends with the formula 'There is no power except through God.'

Key Terms
علم الله
اللوح المحفوظ
اللوح المضيع
الجهال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge leads Him to write conditional statements in the Preserved Tablet, allowing multiple possible outcomes.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's knowledge is certain and unconditional; the Preserved Tablet records only definite outcomes. Conditional statements belong to a 'wasted tablet' representing human ignorance.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the opponent's claim that God writes conditional statements in the Tablet?
A) It is correct because God knows all possibilities.
B) It is the action of one who does not know what will be, and it is not from divine knowledge.
C) It refers to the Preserved Tablet.
D) It shows God's mercy in allowing multiple outcomes.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that God's certain knowledge precludes conditional statements in the Preserved Tablet, which records only definite outcomes; conditional possibilities belong to a 'wasted tablet' of human ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often catch yourself thinking, 'If this happens, then that will happen,' as if the future were uncertain to God? How does Maturidi's argument challenge you to replace conditional worry with trust in God's definite decree?
?
When you face a life decision, do you act as if the outcome is truly unknown, or do you submit to the certainty that God already knows and has written it? What would change in your daily actions if you lived with that certainty?
?
Maturidi says that writing 'this or that' is the action of the ignorant. Where in your own life do you still treat God's knowledge as if it were uncertain, hesitating instead of trusting His written decree? What one situation today requires you to surrender that false sense of possibility?

The Term Is Fixed in God's Knowledge
Maturidi establishes the fixed term in God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the appointed term (ajal) is unchangeable because it is fixed in God's eternal knowledge, and that killing does not alter it.

Core Idea
The term is fixed in God's knowledge; actions do not change it.

Maturidi begins by addressing a potential misunderstanding: that human actions like murder can change a person's appointed term. He clarifies that the term is already known to God and does not change; rather, the action itself is within God's knowledge. This lesson lays the foundation for understanding divine foreknowledge and predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read from 'And His saying: if his term had come' down to 'so likewise is the term'. Notice how Maturidi links the term to God's knowledge.
The first sentence sets up the opponent's claim and Maturidi's reply.
2
Understand the key point
Maturidi says the term is not changed by killing because it is in God's knowledge. Killing happens as God knows it, just like belief and disbelief.
This parallels the way God knows all outcomes, including hypotheticals.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider the example of maintaining family ties: God's knowledge includes what would happen if the person did or did not do it. The term remains unchanged.
This shows God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: لو حضر أجله، فإن أجله ليس يُغيَّر بالقتل فيما كان في علم الله، وهو كما في علمه أنه يقتل، ولكنه بالقتل المنهى عنه أو المأمور به على ما في علم الله، وهو كما في علمه أنه يؤمن ويكفر، فذلك في علمه وكل داخل فيما علم الله عاقبته أنه إلى ماذا يرجع، وإن كان في علمه أنه لو لم يفعل ذلك ماذا تكون عاقبته أنه إلى ماذا يرجع، فمثله الأجل. وعلى ذلك إذ علم الله أنه يصل رَحِمَه فجعل عمره أكثر مما كان في علمه أنه لا يصل، وكذلك أمر الآية؛ إذ محال أن يكون ما يفعله خارجا من علمه، والذي قالوا هو ذلك في المعقول. وللآية قال أهل التأويل: تبين منتهى عمره و [تدل] على نقصان كل وقت يمضى من عمره. وقال قوم: إنما هو في مختلف أعمار الخلق من بين مطوّل ومقصر، لا أن الله يجعل لأحد عمرا ثم تبدو له فيزيد أو ينقص كفعل الجهال ومن في أمورهم على شك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والله تعالى يقول: إذا جاء أجلهم فلا يستأخرون ساعة ولا يستقدمون [يونس، ٤٩/١٠]، وفيما يقول لا يجئ أجلهم بل يُقتلون قبل مجيء أجلهم. والله أيضاً لا يزيد في العمر، وكيف يقدر أن يزيد في عمر آخر بصلة الرَّحِم من لم يقدر بإيفاء ما ضمن [من] عمره أن يبقيه إلى وقت وكذا، بل قدر عدوه حتى منعه عن ذلك. جلّ الله عن هذا الوصف. ثم يقال له: ما ضَرَب من المدة له ألا كان في اللوح أنه يُبقيه إلى ذلك، أو يبقى هو إلى ذلك، أو يُبقيه ويبقى إن لم يُقتل؟ فإن قال بالأول والثاني ادعى عليه الكذب في خبره والخلف في وعده. وإن قال بالثالث قيل: أكان يعلم أنه يُقتل أو لا؟ فإن قال: لا، استحق الإبانة بين رأسه وجسده والخلود في عذاب ربه. وإن قال: نعم، قيل: لِمَ كتب ما لا يعلم؟ إذ ذلك في العرف صنيع الجهال مما تأبى عقول من عرف الرب التفوه به. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم يُعارَض بمن علم الله أنه لا يُقتل ويريدون قتله ويؤثرونه ويقصدون قصده بجميع ما يحتمله وسعهم، ثم يكون على ما علم وهذه أسباب لا تجد أحداً تكون منه لا يقع الفعل به، وفي الوقوع كذبه إلا أن يقول: يمنع، فيلزمه في كل ما يعلم الله أنه لا يكون المنع مع القوة. وإذا لزم ذلك لزم الدفع في كل ما يعلم أنه يكون إذا لم يرض به العبد. فيكون كل خير وشر بالمنع والدفع الذي ظنوا به أن قول خصومهم يؤدي إليه، [و]هو الذي حملهم على رأيهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And His saying: if his term had come, for his term is not changed by killing in what was in God's knowledge, and it is as in His knowledge that he is killed, but it is by the prohibited killing or the commanded killing according to what is in God's knowledge, and it is as in His knowledge that he believes and disbelieves, so that is in His knowledge and everything is included in what God knows its end, that to what it returns, and if it were in His knowledge that if he did not do that, what would be its end, that to what it returns, so likewise is the term. And upon that, since God knew that he would maintain family ties, He made his lifespan more than what was in His knowledge that he would not maintain them, and likewise the matter of the verse; for it is impossible that what He does is outside His knowledge, and what they said is that in the rational. And regarding the verse, the people of interpretation said: It manifests the end of his lifespan and [indicates] the decrease of every time that passes from his lifespan. And some people said: It is only about the different lifespans of creation, between long and short, not that God assigns a lifespan to someone then it appears to Him to increase or decrease like the action of the ignorant and those who are in doubt about their affairs. And there is no power except through God. And God, exalted, says: When their term comes, they will not delay an hour nor advance it [Yunus 10:49], and in what He says, their term does not come, but they are killed before the coming of their term. And God also does not increase lifespan, and how can He increase another's lifespan through maintaining family ties for one whom He did not enable, by fulfilling what He guaranteed from his lifespan, to keep him until such a time, but rather He enabled his enemy so that he prevented him from that. Exalted is God above this description. Then it is said to him: What term did He appoint for him? Is it not in the Tablet that He will keep him alive until that time, or that he will remain until that time, or that He will keep him and he will remain if he is not killed? If he says the first or second, he attributes lying to Him in His report and breaking His promise. And if he says the third, it is said: Did He know whether he would be killed or not? If he says: No, he deserves the separation between his head and his body and eternity in the punishment of his Lord. And if he says: Yes, it is said: Why did He write what He does not know? For that is in custom the action of the ignorant, which the intellects of those who know the Lord refuse to utter. And there is no power except through God. Then he is confronted with one whom God knows will not be killed, yet they want to kill him and seek him and aim for him with all that they can bear and have capacity, then it happens according to what God knows, and these are causes that you will not find anyone from whom the action does not occur to him, and in its occurrence his lie is evident unless he says: He prevents, so it follows for him in everything that God knows will not happen that prevention is with power. And when that follows, then prevention follows in everything that God knows will happen if the servant does not consent to it. So every good and evil occurs by the prevention and impetus which they thought that the saying of their opponents leads to, and it is that which carried them to their opinion. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument about the appointed term (ajal) and its relation to God's foreknowledge and the Tablet. Maturidi begins by addressing the opponent's statement 'if his term had come' and clarifies that the term is not changed by killing because it is fixed in God's knowledge; the killing occurs as God knows, whether it is prohibited or commanded. He then addresses the verse about increasing lifespan through maintaining family ties, explaining that it does not mean God changes His mind but rather that the verse manifests the end or refers to different people. He refutes the idea that God can be uncertain. Then he poses a dilemma about the Tablet: is it written that God will keep him alive until the term, or that he will remain, or both if not killed? Each answer leads to a contradiction: either implying God lies or that He is ignorant. Finally, he presents a counter-argument: if God knows a person will not be killed despite others' attempts, then those attempts fail. Thus, all good and evil occur through God's prevention and causing, which is exactly what the opponents sought to avoid. The passage ends with 'There is no power except through God.'

According to Maturidi, why is the term not changed by killing?
A) Because killing is a free human act that overrides God's decree.
B) Because the term is already in God's knowledge and does not change.
C) Because God changes the term based on whether the killing is prohibited or commanded.
D) Because killing only affects the body, not the soul.

Key Takeaway
The appointed term is fixed in God's knowledge and cannot be altered by any human action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every moment of your life is precisely fixed and known by Allah, would you live differently? How would your attitude toward risk, safety, and fear change? What is one specific area where you act as if your term is under your control, and what would it mean to surrender that to Allah's knowledge?
?
The passage compares the term to belief and disbelief. How does it feel to know that your faith or disbelief is also known to Allah? Does that make you more or less responsible? What does it mean to strive in faith when the outcome is already known?
?
God knows what would happen if you made a different choice. How does that expand your sense of moral responsibility before Allah? Can you think of a situation where you chose wrongly and now realize that Allah knew both what you did and what you would have done if you had chosen differently? How does this affect your view of repentance?

Interpreting the Verse on Increased Lifespan
Maturidi interprets the lifespan verse and refutes change in God's decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse about increasing lifespan through maintaining family ties does not mean God changes His decree, but rather manifests the end of life or refers to different individuals.

Core Idea
The verse does not imply change in God's decree.

Maturidi now tackles a verse that seems to suggest that actions can increase lifespan. He clarifies that it does not imply God changes His mind. Instead, the verse either shows the end of life or refers to different lifespans among people. This lesson guards against anthropomorphic and uncertain conceptions of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the interpretation section
Read from 'And upon that, since God knew that he would maintain family ties' down to 'like the action of the ignorant'. Pay attention to the two interpretations.
Maturidi first mentions an example, then cites two scholarly views.
2
Identify the two interpretations
First interpretation: the verse manifests the end of lifespan and indicates decrease of time. Second: it refers to different lifespans among people, not a change for one person.
Both reject the idea that God alters an already decreed term.
3
Understand the refutation
Maturidi stresses it is impossible for what God does to be outside His knowledge. The idea of God changing His mind is for the ignorant.
This is a strong theological principle: God's knowledge is changeless.

Commentary

Misconception & Correction

?

The Dilemma of the Tablet
Maturidi refutes the opponent using a dilemma about the Tablet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical dilemma: if the Tablet states a specific term, then either God's promise is broken or He is ignorant, both impossible for God.

Core Idea
The Tablet's record must be consistent with God's knowledge and actions.

Maturidi now presents a powerful dilemma. He asks the opponent: what does the Tablet (al-Lawh) say about a person's lifespan? The possible answers lead to contradictions. This lesson teaches how to use logical reasoning to defend orthodox theology against opponents who claim human actions can change the term.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read from 'Then it is said to him: What term did He appoint for him?' down to 'Exalted is God above this description.'
Maturidi presents three possibilities and their consequences.
2
Analyze each option
Option 1: God will keep him alive until that time. If he is killed, God's word is false. Option 2: He will remain until that time. Same problem. Option 3: He will remain if not killed. This leads to a question about God's knowledge of the killing.
Each option is refuted by showing it implies something impossible for God.
3
Understand the refutation of Option 3
If the third option is chosen, Maturidi asks: Did God know the person would be killed? If no, God is ignorant (impossible). If yes, why did He write something conditional when He knew it would happen? That would be like the ignorant.
The logic forces the opponent to admit the term is fixed and not conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: لو حضر أجله، فإن أجله ليس يُغيَّر بالقتل فيما كان في علم الله، وهو كما في علمه أنه يقتل، ولكنه بالقتل المنهى عنه أو المأمور به على ما في علم الله، وهو كما في علمه أنه يؤمن ويكفر، فذلك في علمه وكل داخل فيما علم الله عاقبته أنه إلى ماذا يرجع، وإن كان في علمه أنه لو لم يفعل ذلك ماذا تكون عاقبته أنه إلى ماذا يرجع، فمثله الأجل. وعلى ذلك إذ علم الله أنه يصل رَحِمَه فجعل عمره أكثر مما كان في علمه أنه لا يصل، وكذلك أمر الآية؛ إذ محال أن يكون ما يفعله خارجا من علمه، والذي قالوا هو ذلك في المعقول. وللآية قال أهل التأويل: تبين منتهى عمره و [تدل] على نقصان كل وقت يمضى من عمره. وقال قوم: إنما هو في مختلف أعمار الخلق من بين مطوّل ومقصر، لا أن الله يجعل لأحد عمرا ثم تبدو له فيزيد أو ينقص كفعل الجهال ومن في أمورهم على شك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والله تعالى يقول: إذا جاء أجلهم فلا يستأخرون ساعة ولا يستقدمون [يونس، ٤٩/١٠]، وفيما يقول لا يجئ أجلهم بل يُقتلون قبل مجيء أجلهم. والله أيضاً لا يزيد في العمر، وكيف يقدر أن يزيد في عمر آخر بصلة الرَّحِم من لم يقدر بإيفاء ما ضمن [من] عمره أن يبقيه إلى وقت وكذا، بل قدر عدوه حتى منعه عن ذلك. جلّ الله عن هذا الوصف. ثم يقال له: ما ضَرَب من المدة له ألا كان في اللوح أنه يُبقيه إلى ذلك، أو يبقى هو إلى ذلك، أو يُبقيه ويبقى إن لم يُقتل؟ فإن قال بالأول والثاني ادعى عليه الكذب في خبره والخلف في وعده. وإن قال بالثالث قيل: أكان يعلم أنه يُقتل أو لا؟ فإن قال: لا، استحق الإبانة بين رأسه وجسده والخلود في عذاب ربه. وإن قال: نعم، قيل: لِمَ كتب ما لا يعلم؟ إذ ذلك في العرف صنيع الجهال مما تأبى عقول من عرف الرب التفوه به. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم يُعارَض بمن علم الله أنه لا يُقتل ويريدون قتله ويؤثرونه ويقصدون قصده بجميع ما يحتمله وسعهم، ثم يكون على ما علم وهذه أسباب لا تجد أحداً تكون منه لا يقع الفعل به، وفي الوقوع كذبه إلا أن يقول: يمنع، فيلزمه في كل ما يعلم الله أنه لا يكون المنع مع القوة. وإذا لزم ذلك لزم الدفع في كل ما يعلم أنه يكون إذا لم يرض به العبد. فيكون كل خير وشر بالمنع والدفع الذي ظنوا به أن قول خصومهم يؤدي إليه، [و]هو الذي حملهم على رأيهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And His saying: if his term had come, for his term is not changed by killing in what was in God's knowledge, and it is as in His knowledge that he is killed, but it is by the prohibited killing or the commanded killing according to what is in God's knowledge, and it is as in His knowledge that he believes and disbelieves, so that is in His knowledge and everything is included in what God knows its end, that to what it returns, and if it were in His knowledge that if he did not do that, what would be its end, that to what it returns, so likewise is the term. And upon that, since God knew that he would maintain family ties, He made his lifespan more than what was in His knowledge that he would not maintain them, and likewise the matter of the verse; for it is impossible that what He does is outside His knowledge, and what they said is that in the rational. And regarding the verse, the people of interpretation said: It manifests the end of his lifespan and [indicates] the decrease of every time that passes from his lifespan. And some people said: It is only about the different lifespans of creation, between long and short, not that God assigns a lifespan to someone then it appears to Him to increase or decrease like the action of the ignorant and those who are in doubt about their affairs. And there is no power except through God. And God, exalted, says: When their term comes, they will not delay an hour nor advance it [Yunus 10:49], and in what He says, their term does not come, but they are killed before the coming of their term. And God also does not increase lifespan, and how can He increase another's lifespan through maintaining family ties for one whom He did not enable, by fulfilling what He guaranteed from his lifespan, to keep him until such a time, but rather He enabled his enemy so that he prevented him from that. Exalted is God above this description. Then it is said to him: What term did He appoint for him? Is it not in the Tablet that He will keep him alive until that time, or that he will remain until that time, or that He will keep him and he will remain if he is not killed? If he says the first or second, he attributes lying to Him in His report and breaking His promise. And if he says the third, it is said: Did He know whether he would be killed or not? If he says: No, he deserves the separation between his head and his body and eternity in the punishment of his Lord. And if he says: Yes, it is said: Why did He write what He does not know? For that is in custom the action of the ignorant, which the intellects of those who know the Lord refuse to utter. And there is no power except through God. Then he is confronted with one whom God knows will not be killed, yet they want to kill him and seek him and aim for him with all that they can bear and have capacity, then it happens according to what God knows, and these are causes that you will not find anyone from whom the action does not occur to him, and in its occurrence his lie is evident unless he says: He prevents, so it follows for him in everything that God knows will not happen that prevention is with power. And when that follows, then prevention follows in everything that God knows will happen if the servant does not consent to it. So every good and evil occurs by the prevention and impetus which they thought that the saying of their opponents leads to, and it is that which carried them to their opinion. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument about the appointed term (ajal) and its relation to God's foreknowledge and the Tablet. Maturidi begins by addressing the opponent's statement 'if his term had come' and clarifies that the term is not changed by killing because it is fixed in God's knowledge; the killing occurs as God knows, whether it is prohibited or commanded. He then addresses the verse about increasing lifespan through maintaining family ties, explaining that it does not mean God changes His mind but rather that the verse manifests the end or refers to different people. He refutes the idea that God can be uncertain. Then he poses a dilemma about the Tablet: is it written that God will keep him alive until the term, or that he will remain, or both if not killed? Each answer leads to a contradiction: either implying God lies or that He is ignorant. Finally, he presents a counter-argument: if God knows a person will not be killed despite others' attempts, then those attempts fail. Thus, all good and evil occur through God's prevention and causing, which is exactly what the opponents sought to avoid. The passage ends with 'There is no power except through God.'

Key Terms
اللوح
أجل
علم الله
المنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the problem with the third option (conditional in the Tablet)?
A) It implies God changes His mind.
B) It implies God does not know the future.
C) It implies that God is not in control.
D) It implies that human actions override God's decree.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma shows that if the term were conditional in the Tablet, it would imply ignorance in God, so the term must be fixed and unconditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that God knows everything, including the moment of your death. Does your daily life reflect that trust? How would your anxiety change if you fully accepted that your term is already fixed and known?
?
The passage says that writing a conditional decree is an act of ignorance. When you make plans, do you ever treat God’s decree as conditional—thinking you can change it by your own actions? What would it look like to live with the certainty that His knowledge is perfect and His decree is final?
?
If you were told that your death is recorded in the Tablet as unconditional, what would change about how you spend your next hour? Are there any grudges or attachments you would let go of in light of that certainty?

Divine Attributes
advanced
7 min
The Final Refutation: Prevention and Causation
Maturidi concludes that good and evil occur by God's prevention and causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's final argument that all events, good and evil, occur by God's prevention and causation, refuting the opponent's attempt to avoid divine control over evil.

Core Idea
Everything happens by God's prevention and causation, not by independent human action.

Maturidi concludes his refutation with a powerful counter-argument. He shows that if opponents claim God prevents some events, they must accept that all events are determined by God's will. This lesson reveals the logical consistency of the orthodox position.

Guided Path
1
Read the final argument
Read from 'Then he is confronted with one whom God knows will not be killed' to the end. Focus on the idea of prevention and causation.
Maturidi uses the opponent's own logic against them.
2
Understand the counter-argument
God knows some will not be killed despite attempts. Those attempts fail because God prevents. This proves God's power over all events.
If prevention is possible, then all events are subject to God's will.
3
Contrast the opponent's position
The opponent thought they could avoid tying God to evil, but their own view leads to the same conclusion: everything is by God's prevention and causing.
This is a 'tu quoque' argument: the opponent's position collapses into the same as the orthodox.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That humans can independently cause events without divine involvement, especially evil ones.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even the opponent must admit God prevents some events, so all events are ultimately under God's control.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about the cause of all good and evil?
A) They are independent of God's will.
B) They occur by God's prevention and causation.
C) They are solely due to human free will.
D) They are random and not controlled by God.

Key Takeaway
All events, good and evil, occur by God's prevention and causation, as even the opponent's logic leads to this conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you earnestly desire something and it does not happen, do you truly accept that it is because God prevented it? Or does a part of you feel that it was unfair or that you were capable of achieving it yourself? Reflect on a specific instance and ask: 'What would it mean to genuinely believe that every outcome—including this one—is by His prevention and causation?'
?
If every good and evil that occurs is by God's prevention or causation, then what about the times you have done wrong? Do you blame God for your sins, or do you acknowledge your own responsibility while still affirming His sovereign decree? How do you reconcile the two in your own heart, especially when you feel the weight of your choices?
?
The passage says that even when the servant does not consent to an event, God causes it to happen. Think of something in your life that you strongly disapproved of but which occurred anyway. Did you react with anger or submission? What does your reaction reveal about your real belief in God's absolute sovereignty over all events?

The True Meaning of Divine Will
Maturidi defines true divine will by distinguishing it from other meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of divine will and how it must be distinguished from other concepts like wish, command, and pleasure.

Core Idea
Divine will is not a wish or command; it is the attribute that necessarily brings about its object.

Maturidi begins by clarifying what divine will truly means. He sets it apart from common linguistic uses such as wishing, commanding, or being pleased. This foundational distinction is crucial for understanding God's absolute power and causation in the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of alternative meanings for 'will'.
The passage is concise; each word matters.
2
Identify the alternative meanings
Maturidi lists four other usages of 'will': wish (tamanni), command (amr), claim (da'wa), and pleasure (rida). Understand each one.
These are not the true attribute of God in every context.
3
Reflect on why God cannot be described with these in everything
Ask yourself: why can't God be described with 'wish' or 'pleasure' in all things? Maturidi says it is permissible that He not be described with some in everything, and it would be contradictory otherwise.
This leads to affirming the true attribute of will that is always effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ ذلك معنى حقيقة الإرادة في الشاهد؛ إلا أن يُراد بالإرادة التمنى أو الأمر والدعوى أو الرضا ونحو ذلك، مما يجوز أن لا يوصف الله ببعض ذلك في كل شيء، ويُنقض ذلك في شيء البتة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ويمكن أن تُفرد عن تلك بما أفردها أهل الكلام، وإن كان الحق هو الأول. على أن في إيجاب القول بالإرادة في كل شيء إيجاب القول بخلق الأفعال. مع ما يمكن الاستدلال في هذا بأشياء ليست في الأول، وإن كان في تحقيق الكلام في هذه تحقيق في الأولى. قال الله تعالى: فمن يرد الله أن يهديه يشرح صدره للإسلام إلى قوله : وكأنها يصعد في السماء. أخبر أنه يريد هداية قوم بأفعاله لهم بهدايته، وإضلال قوم بجعل قلوبهم ضيقة حرجة. وقال عز وجل: من يشا الله يضلله ومن يشأ يجعله على صراط مستقيم [الأنعام، ٣٩/٦]، ففرق بين القوم بالمشيئتين. فدلت الآيات على أن الله شاء لكل فريق بما علم أن يكون منهم، ودل على أن المشيئة في هاتين الآيتين ليست بأمر ولا رضا. وقال تعالى: ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها [السجدة، ۱۳/۳۲]، وقال: ولو شاء الله لجعلكم أمة واحدة [المائدة، ٤٨/٥]، وقال: فلو شاء لهداكم أجمعين [الأنعام، ١٤٩/٦]، ولا يُحتمل أن تكون هذه المشيئة رضاً أو أمرا لما قد كانا. ثبت أنه أراد به المشيئة التي يكون عندها فعل لا محالة. ولا يُحتمل أن تكون قد كانت وهو يقول: لو كان [كذا] ليكون كذا، وفي تحقيق الكون دون الموعود به كذب، جل الله عن ذلك.
This is the meaning of the reality of will in the witnessed world, except that by 'will' may be meant wish, or command and claim, or pleasure and the like, such that it is permissible that God not be described with some of that in everything, and that is contradicted in anything at all. And there is no power except with God. And it is possible to single out from that [other meanings] what the people of kalam singled out, even if the truth is the first. Moreover, in affirming the statement of will in everything is affirming the statement of the creation of actions. Along with what can be inferred in this by things not in the first, even if in the verification of speech in this is verification in the first. God Almighty said: 'So whomsoever God wills to guide, He expands his breast to Islam' until His saying: 'as if he were climbing up to the sky.' He informed that He wills guidance for a people by His actions for them with His guidance, and misguidance for a people by making their hearts constricted and straitened. And He, mighty and majestic, said: 'Whom God wills He misguides, and whom He wills He puts on a straight path' [al-An'am 6:39]. He distinguished between the people by the two willings. So the verses indicate that God willed for each group according to what He knew would be from them, and indicate that the willing in these two verses is not command nor pleasure. And He Almighty said: 'And if We willed, We would give every soul its guidance' [al-Sajda 32:13], and said: 'And if God willed, He would have made you one nation' [al-Ma'ida 5:48], and said: 'If He willed, He would have guided you all' [al-An'am 6:149], and it is not plausible that this willing be pleasure or command, because they have already occurred. It is established that He intended by it the willing with which the act necessarily occurs. And it is not plausible that it could have been while He says: 'if it were [such] it would be such,' and in the realization of existence without what is promised there is lying — exalted is God above that.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage defines the true meaning of divine will (irada) by distinguishing it from other meanings such as wish (tamanni), command (amr), claim (da'wa), or pleasure (rida). Maturidi notes that God may not be described with some of these secondary meanings in every context, and they may contradict each other if applied universally. He then asserts that affirming God's will in all things necessarily entails affirming the creation of actions, because true will is that which brings about its object. This is supported by several Quranic verses showing that God's will (mashī'a) for guidance and misguidance is not mere command or pleasure, but a will that inevitably produces its effect. The verses demonstrate that God wills for each group what He knows will occur from them. The passage concludes with the logical point: God's will cannot be something that was and then He says 'if it were such' as if unrealized; that would imply falsehood, which is impossible for God.

Key Terms
الإرادة
التمنى
الأمر

الرضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will is like a human wish or command that may not be fulfilled.
Correction: Divine will is always effective; it necessarily brings about its object. Wish and command do not have this property.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is NOT a meaning that 'will' can take in common usage?
A) Wish (tamanni)
B) Command (amr)
C) Knowledge (ilm)
D) Pleasure (rida)

Key Takeaway
True divine will is distinct from wish, command, and pleasure; it is the attribute that necessarily actualizes what is willed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God willed it,' do you truly believe He actively brought it about, or are you merely accepting something passively? How does Maturidi's definition challenge your everyday speech about God's will?
?
Have you ever thought of God's will as a wish that might not come true, as if God desires but cannot actualize? How does this lesson confront that thought, and what will you do to correct it in your heart?
?
Reflect on a time you attributed something to 'God's pleasure or disapproval' (ridā) without considering His active will. Are you guilty of reducing God's will to an emotion? Commit today to using the term 'will' with the precise meaning Maturidi teaches.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Divine Will Entails Creation of Actions
Maturidi argues that divine will entails creation of actions and cites Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that affirming God's will in all things necessarily entails the creation of actions, and see Quranic evidence that distinguishes God's will from command and pleasure.

Core Idea
If God wills something, it must happen; His will is not a mere command but directly causes existence.

Having established the true meaning of divine will, Maturidi now draws its critical implication: if God's will is real and effective, then whatever He wills must come into being. This directly leads to affirming that God creates all actions. He then supports this with Quranic verses showing that God's will for guidance and misguidance is not just a command or pleasure but an effective volition that produces its result.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Maturidi states: affirming will in everything entails affirming creation of actions. Underline this sentence.
This is the core logical link.
2
Analyze the first set of Quranic verses
Read the verses from al-An'am 6:125 and 6:39. Notice how God distinguishes between groups by His will. Maturidi says this shows the willing is not command or pleasure.
God's will for guidance and misguidance is directly linked to His actions.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Maturidi argues that if God says 'if He willed, He would have done X' and X did not happen, then that 'will' cannot be the true will. True will always actualizes its object.
This refutes the idea that God's will could be ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ ذلك معنى حقيقة الإرادة في الشاهد؛ إلا أن يُراد بالإرادة التمنى أو الأمر والدعوى أو الرضا ونحو ذلك، مما يجوز أن لا يوصف الله ببعض ذلك في كل شيء، ويُنقض ذلك في شيء البتة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ويمكن أن تُفرد عن تلك بما أفردها أهل الكلام، وإن كان الحق هو الأول. على أن في إيجاب القول بالإرادة في كل شيء إيجاب القول بخلق الأفعال. مع ما يمكن الاستدلال في هذا بأشياء ليست في الأول، وإن كان في تحقيق الكلام في هذه تحقيق في الأولى. قال الله تعالى: فمن يرد الله أن يهديه يشرح صدره للإسلام إلى قوله : وكأنها يصعد في السماء. أخبر أنه يريد هداية قوم بأفعاله لهم بهدايته، وإضلال قوم بجعل قلوبهم ضيقة حرجة. وقال عز وجل: من يشا الله يضلله ومن يشأ يجعله على صراط مستقيم [الأنعام، ٣٩/٦]، ففرق بين القوم بالمشيئتين. فدلت الآيات على أن الله شاء لكل فريق بما علم أن يكون منهم، ودل على أن المشيئة في هاتين الآيتين ليست بأمر ولا رضا. وقال تعالى: ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها [السجدة، ۱۳/۳۲]، وقال: ولو شاء الله لجعلكم أمة واحدة [المائدة، ٤٨/٥]، وقال: فلو شاء لهداكم أجمعين [الأنعام، ١٤٩/٦]، ولا يُحتمل أن تكون هذه المشيئة رضاً أو أمرا لما قد كانا. ثبت أنه أراد به المشيئة التي يكون عندها فعل لا محالة. ولا يُحتمل أن تكون قد كانت وهو يقول: لو كان [كذا] ليكون كذا، وفي تحقيق الكون دون الموعود به كذب، جل الله عن ذلك.
This is the meaning of the reality of will in the witnessed world, except that by 'will' may be meant wish, or command and claim, or pleasure and the like, such that it is permissible that God not be described with some of that in everything, and that is contradicted in anything at all. And there is no power except with God. And it is possible to single out from that [other meanings] what the people of kalam singled out, even if the truth is the first. Moreover, in affirming the statement of will in everything is affirming the statement of the creation of actions. Along with what can be inferred in this by things not in the first, even if in the verification of speech in this is verification in the first. God Almighty said: 'So whomsoever God wills to guide, He expands his breast to Islam' until His saying: 'as if he were climbing up to the sky.' He informed that He wills guidance for a people by His actions for them with His guidance, and misguidance for a people by making their hearts constricted and straitened. And He, mighty and majestic, said: 'Whom God wills He misguides, and whom He wills He puts on a straight path' [al-An'am 6:39]. He distinguished between the people by the two willings. So the verses indicate that God willed for each group according to what He knew would be from them, and indicate that the willing in these two verses is not command nor pleasure. And He Almighty said: 'And if We willed, We would give every soul its guidance' [al-Sajda 32:13], and said: 'And if God willed, He would have made you one nation' [al-Ma'ida 5:48], and said: 'If He willed, He would have guided you all' [al-An'am 6:149], and it is not plausible that this willing be pleasure or command, because they have already occurred. It is established that He intended by it the willing with which the act necessarily occurs. And it is not plausible that it could have been while He says: 'if it were [such] it would be such,' and in the realization of existence without what is promised there is lying — exalted is God above that.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage defines the true meaning of divine will (irada) by distinguishing it from other meanings such as wish (tamanni), command (amr), claim (da'wa), or pleasure (rida). Maturidi notes that God may not be described with some of these secondary meanings in every context, and they may contradict each other if applied universally. He then asserts that affirming God's will in all things necessarily entails affirming the creation of actions, because true will is that which brings about its object. This is supported by several Quranic verses showing that God's will (mashī'a) for guidance and misguidance is not mere command or pleasure, but a will that inevitably produces its effect. The verses demonstrate that God wills for each group what He knows will occur from them. The passage concludes with the logical point: God's will cannot be something that was and then He says 'if it were such' as if unrealized; that would imply falsehood, which is impossible for God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does affirming God's will in all things entail?
A) That God commands all things
B) That God creates all actions
C) That God is pleased with all things
D) That everything is a wish of God

Key Takeaway
God's true will is always effective; it necessitates the creation of actions and cannot be equated with mere command or pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's true will always creates its object, then what does that mean for your own perceived failures and successes? Do you truly believe that every outcome in your life—even your mistakes—is directly created by God's will? How does that change how you respond to disappointment or pride? Pause and let the weight of that sink in.
?
You often say 'if God wills' (insha'Allah) for future events. Do you mean it as a mere habit, or do you realize that unless God wills it, nothing happens? This lesson says God's real will cannot be hypothetical. When you pray for something, do you expect it with certainty, or do you doubt that God's will is truly fixed? Examine your heart right now: do you truly entrust the outcome to God, or do you keep a escape plan in your back pocket? Commit to one prayer today where you ask with full certainty that God's will must come to pass.
?
The conditional verses show that God's will is not like your wishes. God does not simply hope; He decrees. What are you holding onto that you think could be otherwise if you just tried harder? Let go of the illusion that your effort alone can change God's decree. Now, instead of striving to control outcomes, strive to align your actions with what pleases God, trusting that His will is already perfect. Write down one area of your life where you need to surrender control to God's effective will.
